# Advanced Tableau Desktop

> If you’re ready to build expert-level data analysis and visualization skills with Tableau Desktop, you’ve come to the right place. This course is a hands-on and project-based dive deep into several advanced Tableau topics, ranging from data relationships and calculations to predictive analytics and dynamic design techniques. See how to apply these tools to real-world business intelligence use case

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop) | 10h | Advanced | 2K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

#### [Course structure and outline](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/course-structure-outline?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/course-structure-outline?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Dustin] Hey everyone. Dustin Cabral here and welcome to Advanced [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop. If you're ready to build expert level data analysis and visualization skills with Tableau, you've come to the right place. This course is a hands-on and project-based deep dive into several advanced Tableau topics, ranging from data relationships and calculations to [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) and dynamic design techniques. You'll be playing the role of a newly hired analyst for Maven Roasters, a bespoke small batch coffee roasting company. Your mission, use advanced analytics and visual design tools to take the company's BI reports to the next level. We'll kick things off by digging into relationships and Tableau's data model, exploring topics like logical versus physical layers, contextual joins, smart aggregation, and performance optimization. From there, we'll introduce a range of powerful visualization and design tools, including custom templates, dynamic formats, animations, parameter and set actions, and geospatial mapping. Last but not least, we'll cover advanced calculations like RegEx and custom level of detail expressions, along with powerful predictive analytic tools like regression, [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md), clustering, and much, much more. Throughout the course, you'll come across unique opportunities to put your new skills to the test, like analyzing employee earnings for the Boston Police Department, building forecast models for the World Health Organization,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/course-structure-outline?u=76281980&t=94)** and designing geospatial [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) to help FEMA identify environmental risks. We've got a lot to cover, so let's dive in.

#### [Introducing the course project](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/introducing-the-course-project?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/introducing-the-course-project?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's jump right in and introduce our course project. Here's the situation. You've just been hired by Maven Roasters, a bespoke small batch coffee roasting company. Your role? Use advanced analytics and visual design to take Maven's BI reports to the next level. Maven Roasters needs a way to track their KPIs, compare performance across markets, analyze category profitability, and identify high-value customers. All you've been given is a folder of [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) files containing information about orders, returns, [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md), customers, and territories. Your objective is to use [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop to create relationships between data sources, design dynamic [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) and visuals, leverage geospatial mapping features, apply advanced calculations to key business use cases, and integrate [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) assets. Now that we've introduced the course project, let's give you a sneak peek of what you'll be creating throughout this course. What you're looking at is the Maven Roasters end state analytic dashboard. Let's dive into the dashboard to go through a few highlights of what you'll be creating throughout this course. On the first page, you'll see that you're going to create high-level key performance indicators. You'll layer more data through other types of indicators, such as info icons and instruction overlays. You'll see that many of the features taught in this course are to help your end users better understand their analytics and to push your analytics to the boundaries of what is capable inside Tableau.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/introducing-the-course-project?u=76281980&t=95)** As we progress through the dashboard, you'll see other key features that you'll be creating, such as geospatial analytics. You'll be creating analytics that can dynamically change based on the radius of your calculations, highlighting various neighborhoods in your data, using polygon, spatial analytics, density maps, and more. You'll also be optimizing your dashboard for use by your end users, such as providing filter menus for them to select from. We'll be building on much of what you learned in the beginner course and providing much more in-depth analytics and functionality for your end users. Many of the features shown will allow you to dynamically change your visualizations, the dimensions and measures used in your visualizations, and even the parameters that control your calculations shown in the views. This will allow your users to stay on one page, but deliver tremendous amounts of analytics in a single space. We'll also be delivering new analytics that are specific and use-case driven, such as year-over-year calculations on the fly from your visualization and index charts that will dynamically change based on the selection that you've provided. In addition, we'll also use real-world use cases to drive our analytics and showcase what Tableau can really do. This includes doing a deep dive into customer analytics, showcasing cohort analysis, customer order frequency, and new customer acquisition. Looking even deeper, we'll dive into the world of predictive analytics inside Tableau, showcasing things like cluster analysis, control charts, trend lines, and forecasts.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/introducing-the-course-project?u=76281980&t=190)** We'll be diving into these topics and more throughout the training. We're excited that you're here. Let's get started.

### 1. Relationships

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's kick things off by talking about relationships in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Relationships are part of Tableau's dynamic Data Model interface, which automatically assigns join conditions between related tables without materializing those tables into a single data source. Now, there's many advantages to the new Data Model that Tableau's developed, but we're going to go through the top three reasons why the new Data Model is a good thing for analysts like you. First, the new Data Model will reduce the upfront data prep by utilizing flexible relationship joins. This means that relationships will push the join to the viz level, allowing for more possible join scenarios to occur from the same source. In addition to reducing the [Data Preparation](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Preparation.md) needed for each source, you're also going to be able to leverage more use cases per source. This will be accomplished by reducing the number of data sources pulled in to answer those questions as a result of those flexible joins we talked about. You'll also be able to preserve all detailed records from a relationship source with multiple tables. Preserving all detailed records while maintaining aggregation standards wasn't possible in the older version of the Data Model. As a result, you'll be able to trust your results much more by leveraging the new relationship model. The new relationship model allows you to retain measures that could be lost or distorted through joins and blends. It will also ensure that measures will not duplicate or overstate due to join clauses. Most of the benefits you see here can be attributed
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/relationships?u=76281980&t=95)** to Tableau moving the join clauses to your Visualization layer rather than upfront in your Data Pane layer. Next up, we'll do a deeper dive into how the Tableau Data Model works and operates.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (5), [Data Preparation](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Preparation.md) (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), means that (1)

#### [Logical and physical layers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/logical-and-physical-layers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/logical-and-physical-layers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've taken a high-level look at [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) relationships and what they're used for, let's take a little bit of a deeper dive into the Tableau data model. Tableau Desktop's data model is split into two distinct layers, the logical and the physical layer. Both layers can be used in conjunction with each other. They're not mutually exclusive, and oftentimes, you'll be using both in your workbooks. It should be noted that whichever layer you're using, your source data should be clearly understood prior to enabling your model. The logical layer is generally more forgiving than the physical layer, however. The logical layer represents the canvas for creating relationships between tables. The physical layer represents the canvas for creating unions and joins between tables. Note that the physical layer is the same data model structure as the original data model prior to the version 2020.2. It should also be noted that the physical layer joins tables together, while the logical layer keeps tables separate, but it defines relationships between those tables. The joins leveraged in the logical layer only happen at the visualization level. You're simply identifying the matching fields inside the data window for your logical layer, not actually calling out which joins should be performed. Now let's dive a little bit deeper into the differences
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/logical-and-physical-layers?u=76281980&t=94)** between the logical and physical layers. Tables dragged into the logical layer are called logical tables. Logical tables are containers for physical tables. Logical tables remain distinct, not merged inside the data source. Noodles connect logical tables together via related fields. Looking at the physical layer, we see that tables dragged here are called physical tables. Physical tables are merged into a single flat table that defines a logical table. Joins and unions connect physical tables together via related fields. A quick pro tip, to access the data model view from before 2020.2, i.e., joins, unions, and Venn Diagrams, simply double-click on the table brought into the data canvas in your logical layer. There, you'll be able to perform your joins and unions just as you have before, so you won't need to leverage relationships. Although, they do have a lot of benefits and advantages that you can leverage inside your analytics in Tableau. Now that we've talked through the high-level differences between the logical and physical layers, let's take a look at the differences between relationships and joins.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (4)
> **Versions:** version 2020 (1), 2020.2 (1)
> **UI Navigation:** double-click (1)
> **Best Practices:** pro tip (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Relationships vs. joins](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/relationships-vs-joins?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/relationships-vs-joins?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now let's dive a little bit deeper into the differences between relationships and joins. When looking at relationships, we leverage logical tables that are displayed using noodles to identify the relationships between those tables. Relationships require matching fields between logical tables. These matching fields are similar to the matching fields you leverage inside joins, but the joins aren't executed at this level. Relationships keep each table separate and only performs joins at the viz-level when needed. Additionally, relationships avoid data duplication through smart aggregation. Joins leverage physical tables that are displayed using Venn Diagrams to identify the joins between those tables. Joins require a join clause and a join type selection, which is similar but not the same as matching fields. Joins will join data into one physical table, which has a specified granularity defined. This means that once a join is performed, you won't be able to go back to the separate tables that were included in that original data window. When leveraging relationships, you would be able to retain the original view of each table inside your matching window. One advantage that joins have is that they can support row-level security and extract filters. This is something that hasn't been integrated into relationships as of yet. Next up, we're going to look at the requirements for leveraging relationships.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1)
> **Definitions:** means that (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Requirements for relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/requirements-for-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/requirements-for-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a quick look at the requirements for enabling relationships. First, dirty data can make relationships more complex to use. Well-modeled data is preferred when using relationships. Try and avoid upfront data source filters when using relationships. These limit [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s auto join-culling features. When using relationships, matching fields must share the same data type. Types cannot be changed in the data interface. These must be done at the database level. Now this is different than the regular join window where you are able to change your types and perform joins. So just be sure that when you're bringing data in to perform relationships, that they are the same type. Tables with many unmatched values and models with multiple fact tables and with multiple dimension tables can make relationships less intuitive. The more complex these fact tables are, the more complex your relationships and how they'll be presented in the view will become. Geographic fields cannot be used to define relationships. Use the physical layer for these types of joins. Now that we've gone through the high-level requirements for creating effective relationships, let's talk about creating a relationship inside Tableau Desktop.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Creating relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/creating-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/creating-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Next up, we're going to look at how to create relationships inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) desktop. Creating relationships is a similar process to creating joins with your physical tables. You'll start by selecting a table from the left hand side and dragging your logical table into the canvas. You'll notice that instead of the Venn diagrams that come up when you do joins and unions in Tableau, you'll get noodles with relationships. The noodles will connect the logical tables, and you'll be prompted to customize the relationship between those tables. The first piece of customization that you'll be looking at is the matching fields between the logical tables. You'll define the matching fields upfront, but you won't define how they're joined. The joins will be performed at the viz level once you create a sheet. You can see here that you can add one or more matching fields in the top part of your edit relationship window. This window will prompt automatically when you select the noodles between the logical tables. In addition to defining matching fields, you can also customize performance options. Note that Tableau defaults the performance options to be the most lenient possible when connecting your data sources. This means that the cardinality and referential integrity are designed to give you the best performance and accuracy to your data possible. Only change these if needed and if you know your data very well. We'll be talking a bit more about cardinality and referential integrity in the next lesson. Also note that relationships are not supported for data source connections, including cube sources, stored procedures, Splunk, [JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md), Salesforce,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/creating-relationships?u=76281980&t=95)** and SAP HANA with the OLAP option chosen. As Tableau releases newer versions of the desktop platform, it will be sure to expand the relationships options to different [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md). Now that we've talked high level about creating relationships, let's go into Tableau Desktop and create a relationship that will be the basis for our workbook going forward. Let's start off by opening up Tableau Desktop or Tableau Public locally on your computer. We're going to connect to the main Maven Roasters dataset that came in your data packet. Let's go over to [Microsoft Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) and navigate to the data source. We're going to select Maven Roasters and click Open. Okay, now that we have our main data source open, we're going to start bringing in our tables into the view and start assigning matching fields. As a reminder, in versions 2020.2 and above, you'll automatically be defaulted to the relationship view. So you won't have to do anything special to get to the relationships, but if you want to get into your physical tables, you can always double click on a logical table to show that same join and union window. Let's go ahead and bring out our first table. We'll be selecting Sales by Store and dragging it out into the view. Next up, let's bring out our calendar table and assign our matching fields. I'll just click and drag my calendar. You'll see the noodle will come out and automatically connect to that new table. Once I release the table, you'll see we have our Edit Relationship window that comes up automatically. Tableau will automatically try and select fields that it finds are matching between the two tables.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/creating-relationships?u=76281980&t=190)** Here, Tableau has chosen Transaction Date, which is the correct field for this particular table. Let's go ahead and add the other tables in one by one and choose our matching fields. Next up, let's bring in the Customer Lookup table. We'll drop that in the view, and we'll make sure it's connecting to the Sales by Store table. Now, let's go down and check out what matching fields Tableau has picked up. It looks like the matching fields are connecting on Customer ID. That's the correct field that we want to use, so we're going to leave that and just exit out. Now let's go on to our next table. We're going to bring in the Employee Lookup table into the view. Drop that in, and we're going to choose our matching fields. Looks like Tableau's automatically matched on Staff ID, and that's exactly what we want. Let's move on to the next table. We're going to go in and pull in Food Inventory. Let's drop that into the view, and then establish the matching field. It looks like Tableau had a bit of a harder time with this table figuring out which one was going to be the matching field between the two tables. Let's go ahead and look for the matching fields that we want to connect between the Sales by Store and Food Inventory tables. We want to match the Store ID and Product ID. So let's go ahead and just search for Store ID, find it and select it in both tables. Go ahead on the right hand side, choose Store ID, and we've got our first matching field. Now we want to add more fields. We'll go ahead and click that Add more Fields button, and we'll search for Product ID. We'll select that on the left hand side
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/creating-relationships?u=76281980&t=282)** and on the right hand side, that's exactly what we're looking for. Let's go ahead and X out of this view. All right, so we're looking good so far, but we have a few more tables to bring in. So let's go ahead and choose Product Lookup, and drag that into the view. Now for Product Lookup, we want to match on Product ID, and it looks like Tableau has picked that up automatically for us. I'm going to X out and move on to the next table. Let's bring in Store Lookup, and we'll drop that into the view. It looks like Tableau has matched on Store ID, which is exactly what we're looking for. Now, you'll notice that we haven't changed any performance options in this view. We're going to review performance options in the next lesson and do a deeper dive on them, but as far as we're concerned, Tableau's chosen the most optimal performance options for us already, and we're going to leverage those going forward. Let's go ahead and rename the data source to just Maven_Roasters, as we'll have other data sources that we're going to leverage throughout the course. We're going to go ahead and click on sheet one so that our data extracts. Just to note that it may take a little bit for your data extract to process the first time through, but once that's done, you should be all set and ready to go.

#### [Optimizing performance](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/optimizing-performance?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/optimizing-performance?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've created our first relationship in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop, let's take a quick look at a how to optimize performance inside the data window. Performance options are settings that define cardinality or uniqueness, and referential integrity, or matching records between two tables within a relationship. You can see in your edit relationship window that you have the option to expand a section called performance options. In this section, you'll be able to modify the cardinality and referential integrity rules. Cardinality determines if Tableau aggregates before or after joining data during analysis. You should choose the many option for cardinality if field values aren't unique, or if you're just not sure. Aggregation in this method will occur before Tableau joins. If you choose one, then field values are unique. Aggregation will in effect occur after Tableau joins. Note that if one is chosen and records actually are not unique, meaning that there may be multiple null records in each of the data sets, duplicate values will occur, and this can significantly impact the values that you're representing in your data, so be careful when changing off the many-to-many option. Tableau defaults to this option on purpose to give you the most flexible and accurate cardinality from the start. In addition to cardinality, you can also modify the referential integrity rules. Referential integrity determines the type of join used
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/optimizing-performance?u=76281980&t=95)** to get dimension values for a measure during analysis. You would choose all records match, if all your values in selected fields have a match in both tables. Joins will be optimized using inner joins, which are faster, but records can be removed or missed due to unmatched values. Note that the all records match treats records as if there were no null records in matching fields. Tableau will choose some records match by default/ Assuming that you don't know every single piece of your dataset, you'll want to perform the outer joins to make sure that you're not missing anything. By default, Tableau will choose the many and some records match options for cardinality and referential integrity. This is to the benefit of the end user who may not know every single piece of their data and want to maintain as much data as possible until they're constructing the view. When you're building your relationships, make sure that you keep cardinality and referential integrity in mind. Next up, we'll be looking at some visual differences between the new data model and the older data model prior to 2020.2

#### [Multi-fact relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-fact-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-fact-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Okay, so now that we've talked about how to create relationships generally in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop, let's dive a bit deeper into certain types of relationships that involve multiple-base data tables. These data models are called multi-fact relationships. A multi-fact relationship model supports loose semantic coupling by introducing the concept of degrees of relatedness and the ability to build a data model with multiple unrelated base tables. Semantic coupling is a term that describes how tightly data is combined together. On one end of the spectrum is joins. Joins bring together tables into a single view that have tight semantic coupling. Relationships are somewhere in the middle because they don't create a single new table, but maintain their separate tables until joins are needed for a particular visualization. Blending is on the loosest end of the semantic coupling model, with separate distinct sources not joined together. Multi-fact relationships are similar to blending in terms of semantic coupling, but keep a single data source rather than multiple separate ones. So why would you want to use multi-fact relationships? Well, let's think about a quick example. on the screen, you can see a relationship model with a base fact table, Sales by Store. Normally, if we'd want to bring in Sales Goals into the dashboard for a comparison point, we'd blend the data since both would be separate data sources with independent fact tables. What we can do with multi-fact relationships is bring in that second fact table and relate it against only those other tables
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-fact-relationships?u=76281980&t=92)** which makes sense. In this case, we want to relate it to the Store Lookup table, which has store ID that we can match against. With this new multi-fact model, we can create visualizations across those tables with relationships without maintaining separate data sources. Tableau will warn us if we put parts of the data model together which don't have a high degree of relatedness. If you're ever having trouble determining which table is a base table and which are shared tables in a multi-fact model, just remember that base tables, AKA, fact tables, are measure-heavy, specific to a particular context, i.e. sales goals, et cetera, and are typically event-based and have foreign keys. Shared tables, on the other hand, are primarily dimension-based and leverage primary keys. Okay, now that we have a good handle on what multi-fact relationships are, let's talk about how to make them. The good news is that they are super easy to create. If we take this data model on the screen, for example, you can see that we have a primary fact table in Sales by Store, with dimensional shared tables like Calendar, Customer Lookup, Food Inventory, and more. To bring in our Sales Targets Goal table as a second fact table, all we need to do is drag the table into the left-hand side of our canvas until we see New Base Table. From there, we can drag from the plus sign on the right of the new fact table to any of the dimensional shared tables that have relationship matches.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-fact-relationships?u=76281980&t=185)** Finally, we'll define our matching fields and performance options, like we normally do with Tableau relationships. Okay, now let's jump into Tableau Desktop and apply a quick multi-fact analysis relationship in our own data model. Note that for the course project, you don't need to do this piece, so please just follow along. Though you are free to test this functionality out on your own. Okay, so now we're in Tableau Desktop and we're looking at our main data model for Maven_Roasters. We want to bring in those Sales Targets, so we'll go ahead and go over to our left-hand side where we can see our tables, and we'll drag Sales Targets until we see this New Base Table option. We'll let go, and it will become a new base table in our data model. We're not done yet. We do need to relate it to our sum of our dimensional tables, so we're going to go ahead and go to the plus sign on the right-hand side of the table. And the first one that we want to relate it to is our Calendar, 'cause we have dates that match here. So we'll go ahead and drag that up until it's connected to calendar. And then we're going to define our relationships. So for this one, we're going to go ahead and find year/month from our Sales Targets. And then we'll go over to our Calendar and we're going to use our transaction date. Perfect. And now we're going to go ahead and also relate Sales Targets to our Product Lookup. So we'll go ahead and join that there. And then we're going to go ahead and choose our relationship keys. So we'll go in here, and we're going to go ahead and choose our Sales Outlet ID, and then we'll go to Product Lookup and we'll choose Product ID.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-fact-relationships?u=76281980&t=278)** Okay, and that's basically it. Now we have a multi-fact relationship model. One of the best use cases for this data set is that now in order to compare our goals to sales, we don't need to bring in two separate data sources and perform blends. We can just bring in the relevant fields out into our chart and construct it from a single source. Now, of course, this is still limited. When you have cases of published data source, you still need to use blending for those until the Tableau data model is allowed to accept published data sources. But for now, you've got a lot more options with multi-fact analysis. There's so much more you can do with multi-fact relationships, but hopefully this gives you a good primer on how to get started with them.

#### [Interface differences (2020.2)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interface-differences-2020-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interface-differences-2020-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we jump into building visualizations using relationships, let's take a quick look at the interface differences for versions of [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) 2020.2 and above and those below. Versions below 2020.2 do not contain the relationships data model. As a result, they won't look the same and interact the same as they do in the later versions of Tableau 2020.2 and above. Let's take a look at the key differences between the later versions of Tableau and those earlier than 2020.2. Let's first look at the data source interface. Prior to 2020.2, the physical layer was the only data layer available in the data source interface. You could perform unions and joins, but relationships weren't available. In 2020.2 and above, you'll have both a logical layer and a physical layer. The physical layer can still be accessed in the newer versions of Tableau just by clicking on any of the logical tables that you bring into the view. The data grid will also operate differently in different versions of Tableau Desktop. Prior to 2020.2, when you complete joins and unions, you'll get one solidified table to view. Inside the data grid, you'll be able to see a preview of that table as a single entity. In versions of Tableau 2020.2 and above, you'll be able to show all records from individual tables selected within your relationship window. That's because these tables are kept separate in your relationship window and only combined when you create a visualization. In addition to the data grid,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interface-differences-2020-2?u=76281980&t=92)** you'll also see changes in the data pane. In versions prior to 2020.2, you'll have dimension and measure sections. These will be clearly delineated by the dimensions and measures headers and grouped as such. In versions 2020.2 and above, you'll have a different grouping structure for your data pane. Your dimensions and measure labels will be removed, and your dimensions and measures will be grouped separately by table. You'll see that the only separation between your dimensions and measures is a thin gray line. Now note that when you drag a dimension or measure above or below that line, the dimension and measure label will show up, but that transition is much more subtle in the versions 2020.2 and above. Viewing data will also look different between the versions. In versions prior to 2020.2, a single combined data source will be available in your data preview. In 2020.2 and above, individual view data previews are available per table. You'll notice that you have extra tabs on the bottom of your view data window. This will enable you to select and view data coming from individual tables regardless of how the view is constructed. Any table that's contributing to the view will be available to look at independently. And finally, your number of records field will no longer be available. This field was used prior to 2020.2 to give you a row count of your dataset, in 2020.2 and above, this field will be replaced by a new count field, which will take your table name
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interface-differences-2020-2?u=76281980&t=183)** and add count at the end of it. This will serve two purposes. First, it replaces the number of records field, but for the particular table that you've selected. Second, it will have some additional properties related to aggregation and showing your dimensions and measures that we'll talk about in the next lesson. Now that we've talked through the high level interface differences between the current and prior versions of Tableau Desktop and the relationships window, let's take a look at the 8 R's of Relationships.

#### [The 8 R's of relationships](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/the-8-r-s-of-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/the-8-r-s-of-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So next up, we're going to dive into the 8 R's of Relationships. The 8 R's of Relationships help to describe the behavior caused by different relationship types. We'll review each of these in the context of contextual joins and smart aggregations. We'll talk a little bit more about each of those in just a minute. First, we'll talk through high level the differences between contextual joins and smart aggregations. The contextual joins that we'll be going through include remain, relevant, retain, recover, represent, and remove. The two remaining R's relate to smart aggregations and they include replicate and resolve. The eight R's represent the rules and semantics of data presentation using relationships. They should be used as quick reminders when constructing views using the relationships data model. While the eight R's may look a little bit intimidating, we're going to walk through an example of each R to make sure that you understand the ins and outs of all the R's of Relationships. First up, we'll take a look at remain.

#### [Contextual joins: Remain](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950026?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950026?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Contextual joins are created and applied at the viz-level and dynamically change the join type based on the combination of dimensions and measures selected. This is a huge departure from the original [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) data model, in that model, you design join types upfront, but in the relationships model, you're actually setting up your joins by bringing out fields into the view. Two of the most important contextual joins that we'll be going through are remain and relevant. Remain will end up looking very similar to what you would get out of a left join. Relevant will look similar to what you get out of an inner join. Both of these joins are generated just by bringing fields out into the view in your normal workbook. The matching fields that you brought in in the beginning will be used as reference points for the joins performed, but won't actually happen until you bring out the fields. much like blending, this will make it so that your joins happen at the sheet level and can be different per sheet, even though you're using the same fundamental data source. Relationships make it so you can dynamically change the joins per sheet based on the needs of the visualization that you're creating. Let's take a closer look at remain, as it's going to be the most common R of relationships that you're going to run into. The rule of remains states that if dimensions in a view are from a single table, Tableau will show all dimensional values even if no matches exist in the measure table. You can think of this as a left join.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950026?u=76281980&t=93)** In the example below, you can see that we've dragged out neighborhood to rows and sales to columns. Since neighborhood is our only dimensional value, you'll notice that we have some null values showing for some of our dimensions in the chart, this is because we don't have matching values in the sales by store table, but since we're using the remain rule, we're still populating all of our dimensions. The relationship leverage in the view is our matching field on store ID. Now, you'll probably note that when we created our initial relationships, the sales by store table was our primary table on the left hand side, but that doesn't necessarily mean that that is the left table on a left join. The primary table on the left join is more defined by the dimension that you're bringing out than the original view that we constructed in the data source view. The most important thing is the matching fields and the dimension that we're pulling out. If we have a single dimension coming from a single table, that becomes the primary table in the left join. The measures that we pull out afterward will get populated for where they match, but where they don't will be null values. Let's jump into Tableau Desktop and take a closer look how we create a remain rule using relationships. Okay, so we're in our Maven Roasters workbook that we created in the prior lesson, let's go to sheet one and name it, "1. Remain," just so we can keep track of the Rs of relationships as we work through them. I'll go over to the left hand side and I'll right click on quantity sold and say create, calculated field.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950026?u=76281980&t=188)** I'm going to take the quantity sold and I'm going to multiply it by the unit price so that I can get the sales value. I'm going to rename this, "Sales," and hit okay. You'll notice that our sales field is tied to our sales by store table, that's because all of our dimensions and measures that we're leveraging inside the calculated field are related to the sales by store table. If we used fields from multiple tables, we would have a measure that would be dropped down into the bottom of our view on the left hand side data pane, because it wouldn't be related to a single table, but multiple. Let's go ahead and create our first view. We're going to go ahead and click on sales and control click on neighborhood, then we're going to go up to show me and choose a bar chart. We're going to close show me and then we're just going to quickly sort our visualization and make sure that we fit the entire view, just so that we can see all of the information clearly. Let's go ahead and show our labels as well so we have an indication of what our values are. Now, the first thing you'll notice is that we have null values over here for these dimensions. We also have an indicator in the bottom right that we have nulls in the view. This view is now representative of the remain rule. Since we have a single dimension from a single table, we're going to get all of those dimensional values and only the measures where they're applicable.

#### [Contextual joins: Relevant](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3954031?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3954031?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The next contextual join that we'll be looking at is relevant. Relevant states that when combining dimensions across tables, it will display only records where matching fields are found between the tables. You can think of this like an inner join. The fundamental point of this rule is that if you take a dimension from one table and a dimension from another table and you put them in the same view without any counts or without any other measures, you're going to get an inner join between those two tables. The inner join will be performed on those matching fields that we defined upfront in the data window. Based on the fields shown in the view, we can see that we have a field from our store lookup table and our customer lookup table. The two fields are performing an inner join on the matching fields that we defined in the data pane. Because the customer lookup only has store ID fields for three stores, we are actually limiting the number of neighborhoods that we can display due to the inner join. Let's dive into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop and try out the relevant rule of contextual joins. Okay, so we're in our workbook and we're looking at our first sheet that we created on Remain. Next, we're going to go ahead and create a new sheet with our new sheet button. #### [Contextual joins: Retain](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/2020020?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/2020020?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The retain rule states that all records from measure tables are always retained. Normally pulling dimensions from different tables creates an inner join, but by adding a measure, we'll be able to show all measure values from that table. You can think of this as almost a left join, but specifically related to measures. If we take a look at our last example where we add in a measure field from one of the tables, we'll be able to see that we've got new null values in our dimensions exposed, and we've got different values exposed in our measures. This is [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)'s way of exposing measures into the view that may have been filtered out based on the dimensions that you've brought out. If we look at the relationships generated from this view, we've got our Sales By Store table coming in with the count value that we've brought in. This is connected to both the store lookup and the customer lookup. We can see that it's connected by the store lookup with the store IDs, and it's connected to the customer lookup by the customer IDs. Let's jump into Tableau and take a look at how we can get retained to work in the view that we've already constructed. So we're back in Tableau desktop. Let's first just duplicate the sheet that we're on so that we can create a new sheet for retain. We're going to right click and duplicate and just rename this sheet to be 3 Retain. Next up, we're just going to go ahead on the left hand side and double click sales by store count. These are those special count fields that we talked about that take the place of the number of records field. Not only do they count the records in the table,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/2020020?u=76281980&t=93)** but when you bring them out into the view, they'll impact the joins that are displayed. Let's double click sales by store and see what happens. Alright, so we've double clicked sales by store and we can see that we still have our neighborhoods and our home stores, but we've got some new records displayed. We can see that they're displayed as nulls for our dimensions, but we've definitely got values for our measure. What we can't readily see here is that we actually have two customer IDs that exist in the sales by store table, which we're bringing the count out from, but don't exist in the customer lookup table, which is where the home store is coming from. Because of the retain rule, since we brought out a measure, it must display all values. So even though we don't have a match in Home Store, we're just going to get null values for that dimension, and it's going to display all of the measures that we need. Again, Tableau is doing this to try and get your aggregations to come out right and make sure your measures are always displaying the correct values. If I were to go ahead and click on one of my nulls, I could view my underlying data and see that my home store's null. Next up, we'll look at the recover rule, which will be a helpful tool for us to recover dimensions that may be filtered out based on the joins that we're creating.

#### [Contextual joins: Recovered](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955030?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955030?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Next up, we'll look at the contextual join rule, recovered. This rule essentially states that you can recover unmatched dimensions by bringing in the respective count field from the table in question. In the below case, bringing in the count store lookup field allows us to view neighborhoods without home stores. Normally, since we have neighborhood from one table and home store from another table, we're going to get an inner join. But if we bring out that count field from the sales by store table where neighborhoods comes from, then we can get all of the neighborhood dimensional values brought back into the view. This is a particularly useful rule when you want to make sure that you have all the dimensions from a particular table in your view. Let's jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop to see how to apply the recovered rule. Alright, we're back in Tableau Desktop and we're just going to go ahead and right click on our retain tab, hit duplicate and rename the tab to be four and recovered. Once we do that, we're going to go ahead and modify the view. Now the main point of recovered is to recover dimensions that are missing from the view based on the join that's being applied. In this case, we have an inner join applied because we have two different dimensions from two different tables, and we want to make sure that we have all the neighborhoods shown in the view, even if they don't have a home store or sales by store. To do that, we just need to figure out which table neighborhood's coming from and bring in the count field from that table. If we go over to the left hand side, we can see that store lookup has neighborhood in it. So all we need to do is go out and drag store lookup count
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955030?u=76281980&t=97)** over to our detail and drop it in. You'll notice right away that now we have all of our neighborhoods displayed, even if they don't have a home store or sales by store. This is one foolproof way to make sure that all your dimensions are displaying for what you want to present. Next up, we'll talk about the represented rule and how it affects unmatched measures.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **UI Navigation:** click on (1)

#### [Contextual joins: Represented](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3952030?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3952030?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Next up we'll be looking at the Represented contextual join rule. Represented states that, "Unmatched measure fields, excluding the count fields that we talked about from each source, will display as null by default, but can be displayed as zero by using the ZN function." In the example below, we've brought out store type and neighborhood from the store lookup table. We've also brought out our quantity sold from the sales by store table. Now, normally our values will display as null based on their represented rule, but we can always wrap a ZN around those unmatched measure values to get it to display at zero. You can see from the relationships leveraged in the view, we have an inner join between store type and neighborhood, but we're also displaying our quantity sold from our sales by store table. Let's jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop and see how the represented rule works firsthand. Now you can see that we have null values represented for our unmatched measures, to clear that up, we can just double click into our quantity field, which is on our marks card and we can wrap a ZN around the entire field. This will go ahead and replace our nulls with zeros. Just remember that this doesn't need to be done for any of our count fields from any of our tables. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3956019?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Removed is the final contextual join rule that we'll be talking about. This rule states that you can use the count field from any table that you want to remove nulls from. All you need to do is choose a value greater than one to remove the nulls without removing all nulls. In the last example, we didn't want to remove all of the neighborhoods, even though the home stores were null. But the removed rule can essentially help us remove null measures from our table without removing all nulls from the view. In the example below, we've taken the count of Sales by Store field and we've applied the greater than one rule. This will remove any null values that we had in our Quantity Sold field. #### [Smart aggregation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/smart-aggregation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/smart-aggregation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Smart aggregation allows measures to aggregate to the level of detail of their pre-join source. With physical layer joins, you have a single table output at the end of your [Data Manipulation](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Manipulation.md). This means that if you've joined tables incorrectly for aggregation, you're going to end up multiplying the records and creating incorrect values that will be represented in your view. With smart aggregation, [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) takes into account which fields you've brought into the view and aggregates based on those. Rather than the matching fields that you lined up in the initial data source, Tableau only considers the fields that you've brought into the view and the tables referenced when it aggregates the measures inside your view. Smart aggregation can both help with intuitive aggregation and performance. With physical layer joins, it may have been necessary to create those joins upfront, but you'd have to work around them after through blending, level of detail calculations and more. With smart aggregation, you won't have to do nearly as much work on the backend after you've created your matching fields. Tableau will be smart enough to aggregate correctly based on the fields that you've brought out. Let's take a quick look inside Tableau Desktop and see how aggregations can differ depending if you use the physical joins or relationships. For this next step, we're going to create a temporary data source, which will show the join that we're going to compare against the smart aggregation. You're going to want to create a new data source connection, choose [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md), and then navigate to your Maven_Roasters data source.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/smart-aggregation?u=76281980&t=93)** Click open, and we'll create our joins. First up, we're going to drag out our sales by store table and drop it into the view. Next, we're going to double click on sales by store to get to the physical layer. Then we're going to drag out employee lookup and create a join. We're going to do a left join on staff ID. Let's go ahead and choose left and make sure that staff ID is selected. Next we're going to drag out food inventory. Drop that in the view, and we'll get another join clause. We're going to click on the join clause and make sure that we're doing a left join on product ID. Now if you know the data being brought into the view, you can already tell that this is going to cause some aggregation issues. We've got our sales by store, which obviously has multiple orders with multiple [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) on it throughout time. We're joining that to the employee lookup and food inventory tabs, which obviously have those same product IDs on them, but we're going to end up replicating records in our sales by store table. This is unavoidable with this type of joining and will lead to aggregation issues, mainly inflating our dataset. We'll take a look at what this actually does once we get back into the view, but for now, let's X out of the physical layer. Because these joins aren't ideal, we're going to avoid creating an extract, and we're just going to use a live connection for our testing. If you're using Tableau Public, you can simply click on one of your sheets and then X out of the extract option when we get to it. If you're using Tableau Desktop,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/smart-aggregation?u=76281980&t=186)** you can just click the live button, which is on the top right hand side. If you're using Tableau Public, it's going to give you a warning that your dataset is too large to be extracted for Tableau Public. That's okay. We're just going to delete this data source after we've done the test, and all you need to do is click on a new sheet and then hit cancel. You'll notice that this says it's going to create 350 million records, which is not what we want, so let's go ahead and cancel, and we'll get a temporary live connection to this data source. If you're using Tableau Public, you won't be able to publish a live data source anyway, but we're just using this for testing. Now let's rename the data source as Maven_Roasters_Join. We're going to right click on the data source, choose rename, and we're just going to call this Maven_Roasters_Join so that we remember which data sources are joined and which data source is our relationship. Now we're going to go ahead and select two fields from our view and create a visualization. We're going to go and choose store ID and sales and create a bar chart. Let's go ahead and just click on store ID and control click on our sales field. In our join view, we're just going to create a quick calculation, right click and choose create calculate field, and we're going to create our sales field. We're just going to go ahead and take our unit price and multiply that by our quantity sold. Let's click okay, and we'll be selecting our sales field and our store id. If we can just control click on both of those, go over to show me and create a bar chart.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/smart-aggregation?u=76281980&t=280)** Now this might take a little bit. Our dataset is now 350 million records, and it's a live connection, but as we said before, this is just for demonstration purposes. You would never do this in an actual scenario for building a dashboard. All right, it looks like our view finally rendered, and it also looks like we have a much exaggerated value for our sales, and this is because we pulled in our join upfront, and we're joining in a non-optimal way. If we go ahead and create another sheet from our Maven_Roasters dataset and choose store ID and sales, we can see the values that we'll aggregate will be correct. Let's go ahead and create a new sheet and just go over to our store ID. Let's go over and click on our Maven_Roasters data source. Select our sales and our store ID fields and click show me and bar chart just like we did with our join dataset. Now when we reveal our labels, you'll clearly see that our aggregations are correct and not nearly as exaggerated as we had with our joins. You can see how joins can be problematic with aggregation and how we might have to solve them with tools like level of detail calculations, but with smart aggregations, we can get a much better result most of the time. Next up, we're going to dive into the two types of smart aggregation, which include replicate and resolve.

#### [Smart aggregation: Replicate](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951032?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951032?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The first smart aggregation rule that we're going to talk about is replicate. Replicate states that measures will replicate across lower levels of detail in the viz only. This essentially means that we're not replicating the values at row level. We're just replicating the values visually in the visualization that we've created. In the example below, you can see that we've got neighborhood and gender. If we had done a true join upfront, instead of using relationships, we would've replicated our food inventory value across gender. Because there's extra lines for gender that also have neighborhood in our join, we're going to replicate our inventory. This will greatly inflate our values. Since we're using smart aggregation with our relationships, it's just going to replicate that total value for our food inventory across each of these records. This eliminates the fundamental problem with joins and aggregation where we'd have to replicate those values at row level and then add them up inside the view. If we look at the relationships leveraged in the view, we can see that we have our sales by store field and it has matching fields on store ID to store lookup and customer lookup and customer ID to food inventory. We actually don't have any fields in the view from our sales by store table. But [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is using those fields as referential joins to be able to connect the store lookup, customer lookup, and food inventory tables. Remember that with the join, a many-to-many relationship between store lookup and customer lookup tables
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951032?u=76281980&t=94)** would multiply the food inventory values to incorrect proportions. Relationships will replicate those measure values in the view only. Let's dive into Tableau desktop to apply the replicate rule of smart aggregation firsthand. Alright, we're back in Tableau desktop, and the first thing we can do is, just delete the tabs that we created in the last lesson because we won't need to reference 'em anymore. We can also go ahead and close our Maven Roasters data source as we won't be leveraging it anymore, and it is quite large. Let's create a new sheet and name it number seven, replicate. Next up, we're going to select our fields. First, we're going to select gender from our customer lookup table. We'll just scroll up and select that. Then we're going to go down to our food inventory table and we're going to control select on our food inventory count, and we're going to go down and control select on neighborhood from our store lookup table. Then we're going to go over to show me and we're going to create a bar chart. We'll click off of show me and we'll add some labels to our view. We'll also change our view to fit the entire height. Note how the food inventory count replicates across the gender field since that split out is not applicable to this measure. Notice how this is only happening inside the view and not at row level. So we're not having any over aggregation issues within the view. Next up, let's take a look at the Resolve Smart Aggregation rule.

#### [Smart aggregation: Resolve](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951031?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951031?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Our second Smart Aggregation Rule is Resolve. Resolve is closely-related to the Replicate Rule. This rule states that it will resolve aggregations to the measure's native level of detail. What that essentially means is that if you put subtotals in a view, it's not going to aggregate incorrectly. It won't add up those replicated views that we created in the last lesson. With a regular join, this would be even more extra aggregation that isn't native to your actual data source. First, we have extra aggregation because of our join. If we added subtotals to a regular, improperly joined view, it would actually exacerbate the problem of over-aggregation even further. With the Resolve Smart Aggregation Rule, it will resolve the aggregations to the measure's native level of detail, so it won't over-aggregate. The relationships leveraged in this view are the same that we used in the Replicate view. All we've done here is at a subtotal, and you can see how [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) has properly done its subtotaling and not over-aggregated. Remember, subtotals resolve to the measure's native aggregation, which is the Food Inventory Table in this example, and they do not aggregate replicated values across dimensions. Let's jump into Tableau Desktop and see how we can leverage Resolve in our view. All right, we're back in Tableau Desktop, and we're just going to right-click on our number 7 Replicate, choose Duplicate, and rename this tab.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951031?u=76281980&t=93)** We're going to call it 8 Resolve. Next up, we're going to go to our Analysis dropdown, we'll go down to Totals and we'll say Add All Subtotals. Notice how our subtotals are added in, but our aggregations are not combining our aggregations from our replicate view. This is because the native level of detail for this view is coming from the Food Inventory tab, and we don't want to over-aggregate based on this view, 'cause it wouldn't make sense. That's where Resolve and Replicate can help us out.

As a reminder, the rule of replication says that it will replicate measures across lower levels of detail in the vis only.
>
> **[10:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/solution-relationships?u=76281980&t=657)** You can see here, for example, the author Carolyn Segal. She has 1,489 ratings across all of her titles, but we can see that the 1,489 is actually replicated across different formats. If these datasets were joined, we would have an inflated number of ratings, but due to the rule of smart aggregation and replicate, we won't have that. We'll just have that repeating value placed on the vis wherever it's needed. Next up, we're going to talk about the smart aggregation rule of resolve. We'll go ahead and just right click on a Replicate tab and choose Duplicate. We'll rename this field to be Resolve and we'll modify the view slightly. Let's go ahead and just go to Analysis, Totals and Add All Subtotals. We can see here again that our subtotals are not exaggerating our values. Rather, they're taking the context of the view into consideration and aggregating to the correct level. Resolve states that values will resolve aggregations to the measure's native level of detail. Now that you've completed the relationship section of the course, we'll be moving on to the dynamic design section.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (5), [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) (2), [Rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) (1)
> **UI Navigation:** click on (5), go to (2), select the (1)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/dynamic-design?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Welcome to the dynamic design section of the course. Dynamic design covers areas of dashboard creation that fall beyond basic sheet construction and interaction used in standard [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md). To make your dashboard design stand out, we'll focus on visual appeal, flexibility, and interactivity. We'll cover ways to elevate the visual appeal of your dashboard using both external design tools and dynamic visual techniques. We'll look at ways to increase our analytic flexibility by swapping visuals, measures, and dimensions to facilitate flexible analysis and keep users engaged. And finally, we'll look at interactivity. We'll leverage set and parameter actions to enable deeper analysis and app-like interaction with your visualizations. The dynamic design topics that we'll cover will elevate your visual appeal, increase the flexibility of your dashboard, and enable dynamic interactivity for your end users.

#### [Visual appeal](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/visual-appeal?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/visual-appeal?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Visual appeal creates an engaging platform for users to explore and interact with your dashboard. While analytics should be the backbone of your work, visual appeal can drive user adoption as well as comprehension of your analytic vision. We'll cover visual appeal in two main sections. The first revolves around background templates. Background templates provide visual frameworks for developers to build [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md). Templates are both creative and practical tools for design. Note that templates don't refer to chart templates, but rather visual guides to overall dashboard design. The second piece of visual appeal that we'll be diving into relates to information indicators. Information indicators come in many [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md), including KPI shapes, info icons, and instruction and commentary, popups. These devices communicate complimentary data to better inform consumers of dashboards.

Templates can speed up development time and increase visual appeal for your analytic tools. Background templates are created using both internal and external tools. In most cases, people have access to [PowerPoint](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20PowerPoint.md), which is why we'll be demonstrating it in this lesson. But if you have something else like InDesign or another [Graphic Design](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Graphic%20Design.md) tool that you're familiar with, please feel free to use that. The two major steps in creating background templates involve creating the visual background in a tool like PowerPoint, saving it as an image, and then creating your [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) sheets in your workbook. Once this is accomplished, you can combine those two pieces of work into one holistic dashboard with a background image and sheets overlaid. Note that you can overlay sheets on top of a background image using containers that are floating, so for those that are more comfortable with a tiled dashboard, you'll be able to leverage containers that float over a background image so that you can develop in your comfort zone. A handy trick when you're developing images in PowerPoint is that one inch in PowerPoint is equal to 100 pixels in Tableau. So if you have a 1,400-by-1,400-pixel dashboard that you've developed in Tableau, that's a 14-inch-by-14-inch slide in PowerPoint. Once you create your background image in a PowerPoint slide, all you need to do is save it as an image

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/instructions-commentary?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Next up, we're going to be discussing instructions and commentary. Instructions and commentary provide end users with helpful reference points to better utilize and understand your dashboard. Now, instructions and commentary aren't just for advanced [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md), like the ones that we're creating throughout this course. They can be used to display simple information about the interactivity and usability of your dashboard, no matter what analytics you're presenting to your end user. Much like any apps today that you use on your phone or anytime you log in to a website that's recently been updated, they'll give you a walkthrough of the new features and what they do and you can always X out over the overlay and say, "I don't need that information," or you can cycle through it and understand the updates that have happened. So we're going to do something very similar with our dashboard, and it's pretty straightforward but not that intuitive if you've never done it before. It may be deceivingly simple looking, so we want to give them a heads up as to what they can do and that's really what the instructions and commentary overlays can do for us. What do those look like? >

#### [Flexibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/flexibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/flexibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Next up, we're going to dive into flexibility. Flexibility provides developers the ability to create layered analytics within a dashboard, both in the visualizations themselves and supporting filtering and complimentary content. Two fundamental concepts in flexibility are field swapping and containers. Field swapping allows users to dynamically change fields being displayed inside a visualization. Note that field swapping won't change the chart type. It will actually change the pills that are placed on rows and columns and within your detail shelf. This will allow the end user to dynamically change your dimensions and measures within a visualization, and therefore, consolidate many other visualizations that they might have had to create. And you can think of this as taking the place of creating a [PowerPoint](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20PowerPoint.md) slide deck with 10 different versions of the same chart, but with different measures and split by different categories and dimensions. Instead, you'll create field swapping flexible measures and dimensions that can take the place of many visualizations. In addition to field swapping, we also leverage containers for enhanced flexibility. Containers allow users to swap out entire visuals or reveal additional filters and information. We've already done this once with our information and overlay section, and we'll do it again with a few more options, including the entire visual swap out and a filter menu that shows and hides. Now, in this case, we're mainly leveraging our containers
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/flexibility?u=76281980&t=93)** to switch out our visualizations, and you want to leverage your containers for the swapping when your whole cell is changing out the type of visualization. If you're just changing out dimensions and measures and it's going to be the same bar chart or pie chart or whatever it is, then field swapping will work well. But in the case that you want to change out a visualization and you want to have visualizations layered in the same space of your dashboard, then you're going to leverage containers for that. Now, as we mentioned, there's a lot to flexibility in [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md), and this entire section is dedicated to enhancing the dynamic nature of your dashboard and giving the end user many flexible options for consuming their information. Don't forget that the user attention span is relatively short, and we want to keep them engaged with the dashboard at all times. These flexible measures will help them change out dimensions, measures, visuals, information, and filters all in the same space. We're essentially working to consolidate our dashboard into a very usable, succinct space that is both dynamic and flexible for our end user. So next up, we're going to dive straight into multi-dimensions and multi measures, which we'll take into account our field swapping flexibility.

>

#### [Multi-dimensions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-dimensions?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-dimensions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Our first section related to flexibility and field swapping is going to be multi-dimensions. Multi-dimensions enable users to dynamically swap out dimension fields used in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) visualizations. These can be typically created in three simple steps. First, you'll create a parameter that will allow the users to select the desired dimension to display. Note that in our use case we're going to use an integer as the actual value, and this will be for faster performance reasons. It takes Tableau a little bit longer to identify matching string values than it does integer values. Step two, we'll create a calculated field. The calculated field will use a CASE statement driven by the previously created parameter. Note that IF statements can be used, but CASE statements are often the best for this particular use case. The reason is that their format is slightly different and it allows you to create a list a little bit easier than it does if you're using an IF statement. Step three is to create your visualization. You'll create your visualization leveraging the multi-dimension that we just created, and you'll also expose the parameter to the end user so they can select which pill they're going to swap out in that calculated field. The visualization itself will look pretty normal. Nothing to the end user will be apparent that there is a multi measure being used, but they'll have that dropdown selection so they'll be able to choose which dimension they want to show. Now, as a reminder, multi-dimensions and multi-measures are to be utilized with the same visualization type. It allows you to have flexibility in the pills
