#### [Multi-measures](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so the second piece of our field swapping flexibility type is multi-measures. Now, multi-measures will enable users to dynamically swap out measures used in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) visualizations. These will be created very similarly to our multi-dimensions, but there are some differences between multi-dimensions and multi-measures and what you should be concerned about when you're creating them. Now the first step is just the same as multi-dimensions. You're going to go ahead, you're going to create your parameter to allow users to select their desired measure to display. You're going to leverage an integer for faster performance, but you second piece will be slightly different. Now you're still going to do the calculated field with a case statement, but there's going to be a little difference in terms of aggregation. Now, depending on the measures that you're changing out, you may not need to add an aggregation in your calculated field. For example, if you were just swapping out sales and profit, you probably wouldn't need to embed an aggregation at all because when you drag out your multi-measure field, you'd probably want to be using the SUM aggregation. The key point here is that you can only apply one type of aggregation to a pill if you do it after you drag it into the view. So if I was just going to drag multi-measure out and it didn't have a native aggregation, I could only do one at a time. Now with the multi-measures that we're going to be swapping out, we've actually got differences in aggregation. In this example that you can see here, we've got sales and quantity sold both being SUMed, which is the same,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=94)** that would be fine if we only had those two, but we actually have the COUNTD of Customer Id. Now that wouldn't work for us, right? If we were just going to leave the aggregation off here and we got to Customer Id, we wouldn't want to summit, just like we wouldn't want to do a count distinct of sales. So it wouldn't really work. So we're actually embedding our aggregation inside our calculated field, and that works just fine as long as we're aggregating every piece of it. Obviously, you know from the beginner course that you can't mix aggregate and non-aggregate values. That won't work in a calculated field. So we're just going to apply our aggregation inside, but that will also change, you know, how we want to display our values and things like that. But just know that with multi-measures, you do have that extra wrinkle to worry about. You need to think upfront how you want your aggregations to perform. And you need to make sure that if you're doing an aggregation, you do it to everything 'cause otherwise the calculated field won't work. And the third piece, the visualizing is pretty much the same as we did before. You're just going to leverage your multi-measure field in your visualization. You're going to expose your parameter and you'll have the end user be able to change out the different measures that they want to display. Now, in the next lesson, we'll go through an extra step that you might want to take if you're leveraging multi-measures around formatting for your different measure types and things like that. That will be something extra that you might want to consider because if you've got different types of measures, you might want to display them differently, some with dollar signs, some with decimals, some in percent form, and things like that.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=187)** And that gets a little tricky with a multi-measure. So we'll talk through that in the next lesson, but in this lesson, we're going to dive into Tableau Desktop, create our multi-measure, and then integrate it into the visualization. All right, so we're back in Tableau Desktop, and now we're going to create our parameter. So we're going to right-click in our white space, say Create Parameter, and we'll call this parameter Select Measure, just like we did with Select Dimension. We'll change this to integer and we're going to make a list. Our list will be three items long, so we'll go 1, 2, 3 in our list. And our first measure, it's going to be Sales. Our second measure will be Quantity. Our third measure is going to be Customers. And we'll just click OK on that. Now we've got our parameter that's going to drive our calculated field, let's create our calculated field. So we'll just right-click in the white space again, Create Calculated Field, and we're going to call this Multi-Measure. All right, so next up, we're going to create our case statement. So we're just going to say CASE, then we're going to say Select Measure. And then we're going to say WHEN 1 THEN, and we'll do our first measure. So in this case, we want sales, but we are going to embed our aggregations here. So we're going to do SUM, put Sales in there. And that will be our first option. We're going to say WHEN 2 THEN, and we'll do a SUM as well for Quantity Sold. And then we'll have our third option. So WHEN 3 THEN, and we're going to actually do a COUNTD,
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=279)** which is account distinct, and we're going to do that off Customer Id. And that will basically give us the distinct count of customers, and that will be an important measure for us to integrate here. So next up, we're just going to END the clause. And now we've got, in the case of our Select Measure when it is 1, then we want sales displayed, when it is 2 then we want Quantity Sold, when it is 3 then we want the count of Customer Id. So that's pretty much it. We're good on our multi-measure there. We can hit OK, and you'll see the field pop out into the measure window. Now the multi-measure is super powerful on its own. You'll notice that unlike some of the other fields that we've created, this one isn't time bound. So it's useful for trend lines and places where we're going to filter the visualization using the filter shelf. But if we actually want to have the filters built in for the time period, we're going to want to create fields very similar to the way that we did our this year, year to date, our last year, year to date, our year-over-year calculations, but for our multi-measure. So this is really combining a lot of different pieces. It's combining our time flags with our multi-measures. It's combining that with our multi-dimensions. It's making a really, really flexible space for us to be able to change out a lot of different pieces and make it super easy for the end user to be able to customize what they're looking at. So let's go ahead and create those extra calculated fields that we can leverage in different ways for different views. First up, we're going to create our multi-measure this year, year to date.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=372)** And you probably remember how we do that, but we right-click and Create Calculated Field. We call this Multi-Measure this year, year to date. And then we're going to do our case statement. So in this case, we're going to say CASE Select Measure. We're going to say WHEN 1 THEN. And we're going to still do a SUM, but instead of our regular sales field, we're going to use one of our time bound fields. So we're going to say sales this year, year to date. So now it's pulling sales this year, year to date, but for the specific multi-measure that we're selecting. So we're going to do the same for our other multi-measures. We're going to say WHEN 2 THEN, and we're going to do a SUM here, and we're going to do our Quantity Sold this year, year to date. And then we can say WHEN 3 THEN, and we're going to do our Customer Count. Now that one's already aggregated, so you're not going to see it in this view, but we're already doing the customer count COUNTD inside this calculation. So it won't look like it's an aggregate, but it really is. So we'll just go down and we'll use the little arrow key to arrow down to the calc we want, and it's customer count this year, year to date. And then we're just going to say END, and that will be our calculation. So that's looking good. Let's hit OK on that. And then to just cheat a little bit and get by this a little faster, we're just going to right-click on that field that we just created, the multi-measure this year, year to date, hit Duplicate, and then we can just edit that field. So first thing, we're going to edit the title, we're going to say last year, year to date instead.
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=467)** And then we're going to change out just instead of TY, we're going to do LY for all our calculations. And we built these in a very similar way so it's really easy for us to just go in and change that one little letter. And then we've created our calculated field. So it makes it much easier to just kind of replicate this stuff. It might seem like a lot of work when I say, "We need to create four case statements for this to really work." And you're like, "Ah, I don't really want to do that." There's ways to get around it, right? We can duplicate our fields, we can use very similar naming conventions so that we can just change out a few things there and make this a lot faster for ourselves. And like I mentioned before, this stuff is a little bit of work upfront, but it's going to make it way easier for you to develop and make your dashboard really dynamic. So let's hit OK on that. So now we've got this year and last year, and next up we're going to do our multi-measure year-over-year difference. So let's just right-click, Create Calculated Field. We're going to go and choose Multi-Measure YOY Diff as our name. And then we're going to do our case statement. So we'll say CASE Select Measure WHEN 1 THEN Sales YOY Diff. So we can just use that prebuilt diff field that we created. Then we can do that for the other two. So we can say WHEN THEN, and we'll do Quantity Sold YOY Diff. And then we can do that same thing with our third one. WHEN 3 THEN, and we'll do Customer Count YOY Diff, and we'll do END there.
>
> **[9:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=563)** And then we've got one more we want to make. We want to make our multi-measure year over year difference percent, Multi-Measure YOY Diff %. And then we can do our case statement again, can say CASE, and we'll do Select Measure, WHEN 1 THEN, and we'll do our Sales YOY Diff %. And like I mentioned before, as long as your aggregations are the same, it's going to work out well. We've definitely got aggregations built into those sub calculations, so we don't need to worry about it, it just won't look like it's an aggregated field right now. So let's go ahead and we're going to bring in Quantity Sold YOY Diff %, that's right there. And then we'll do our third, which is customer so we're going to do Customer Count YOY Diff, and then we'll do an END there. And now we've got our fully nested dynamic multi-measure fields for multiple time periods and things like that. So we've got a lot here, a lot available for us to play with. So in this case, we're going to want to bring in our Multi-Measure this year, year to date field, and we're going to just drop that right over our sales field, which is on our column shelf, and that will replace it. So let's go ahead and find that. So we want Multi-Measure this year, year to date. We're going to drag that over our columns. We're going to replace our sales, and we're just going to sort this descending. There we go.
>
> **[10:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/multi-measures?u=76281980&t=654)** All right, so that's fully replaced out our measure here. We can try this out by just right-clicking Select Measure, Show Parameter, and then we'll be able to change out our measure. So instead of sales, I might want to show quantity. Instead of quantity, I want to show a count of customers. So I can dynamically change that out and it will change the visualization. You can kind of tell here that we probably want to do a little formatting, but the tough piece is that we don't want to change the format on this pill because that will change this to dollars, let's say for sales, but it would also change it to look like dollars for quantity and customers. So in the next lesson, we're going to actually go through a really cool pro tip about dynamic formatting that is very applicable for multi-measures. But if you look at what we have here, we've actually got five dimensions we can change out, and we've got three different measures that we can change out. So if you multiply those across, we have 15 different charts that can create out of this one chart, and that takes up just a single space in our dashboard. So it's really packing a ton of power, a ton of information into one space. And the end user can customize this however they want, whenever they want, and be able to see that data readily. Next up, we'll dive into our dynamic formatting pro tip.

#### [Pro tip: Dynamic formatting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-dynamic-formatting?u=76281980)

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-dynamic-formatting?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Next up we're going to go through a quick pro tip on dynamic formatting. Now this pro tip is helpful for many different applications, but it's particularly helpful for our multi measures. Multi measures will add complexity due to their mixed formatting, i.e. swapping between sales and profit percent. Some of those you'll want to display in dollars, maybe in thousands, and some of those you'll want to display as a percent with several decimal places. So it can be pretty tricky to do that in one measure field, and it's almost impossible to do with just the native formatting that [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) gives you. To pull off our dynamic formatting, we're going to leverage some additional multi-select labels and aggregation calculations that can be used to customize our formats. The first formats we're going to look at are pre and post labels. Now when we created our multi measure, that was actually the middle of what we want to display. So we need something before such as a dollar sign, and we need something after. And the after piece, we call it the post label. It actually will display if you're changing your aggregations. So if I want to show values in thousands or millions or billions, then I'm going to want a little K or an M or a B after my value is displayed. In some cases, we're not going to need a pre or a post and that's fine. These are all going to be string values, so we can just leave those blank with two quotes, and that will take care of it for us. Now the other piece of dynamic formatting
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-dynamic-formatting?u=76281980&t=93)** is related to dynamic aggregation. So you'll get end users who need to see values in thousands or millions or billions, but maybe they want to be able to change that, and maybe they need to be able to change dynamically based on the values being displayed. There's a lot of complexity there that can happen. And these are particularly useful, especially in KPIs up at the top of your dashboard. If you've got limited space, you might need to do some dynamic aggregation. #### [Sheet selectors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/sheet-selectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/sheet-selectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sheet selectors allow developers to change visuals within the same physical space in a dashboard. This is in direct contrast with the multi-dimensions and multi-measures that we just created. Instead of changing the fields out in a visualization, we're going to wholesale change out the visualization itself. Now, as a reminder, sheet selectors are most useful when you're changing chart type. If you're just changing the fields inside of your visualization, but fundamentally it's the same visualization, then you should stick to multi-dimensions and multi-measures. If you're changing a bar chart out for a map, then that is a good use case for a sheet selector. Sheet selectors are especially useful to maintain user engagement and to keep dashboard space clean and focused. [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md) can become quickly cluttered with too many visualizations and too much going on, and this can end up leaving your user confused and overwhelmed. Sheet selectors can help you create many different pieces of analytics for your users to interact with, but have them layered in the same space. This will allow you to keep the user on the page and keep them engaged while also providing a myriad of options for their analysis. Now there are a few key pieces to sheet selectors. The most common sheet selectors are created using containers. Now there's different drivers that can drive the containers. The example that we're going to go through today will be parameter driven. And essentially the main steps are you're going to create a parameter,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/sheet-selectors?u=76281980&t=93)** then you'll create a calculated field. You'll put that calculated field on a filter shelf, and then from there you'll have both of your sheets or as many sheets as you need contained inside the container and that filter will be driven to show or hide a sheet. Now what does that look like practically? Well, you'll have your outer container, which contains all of the sheets that you need to swap out. You'll have your compressed sheets, which will actually shrink down to one pixel if this is done right. So the end user really won't be able to see where these sheets go when your parameter driven sheet selector is activated. They'll simply compress to one side or a top or a bottom of your container when they're not in use. And of course you'll have your parameter selector somewhere on the dashboard for the end user to interact with, and that's simply pushing to your calculated field, which is in turn affecting your filter. Now that we've looked at sheet selectors high level, let's jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop and create some of our own. Alright, so now we're back in Tableau Desktop and we're looking at our bar chart multi-dim/multi-measure page. >

So in this case, I really don't want anything real on my rows or columns affecting my visualization. So I'm just going to go ahead and create a dummy field. I'm going to right click, say create calculated field. I'm just going to call this blank and I'm just going to put two quotation marks in it. It's really nothing at all, but it's going to allow that dimension factor to be fulfilled. So I'll take that blank. I'm just going to go ahead and drop that on rows. The last piece you need to know about these containers and the sheet swapping and things like that, we need to remove our titles. If you need a title, you need to to create a separate sheet or a separate text box for your title. They really don't work with this compression methodology inside containers. So let's go ahead and just right click on our titles and just say hide title. We're going to do it for both sheets and you'll see that our compression happens right away. So our treemap is compressed on the right hand side and our bar chart is shown. Now for the bar chart, I'm just going to change the height a little bit on this. We'll get into formatting a little bit more later, but I'm just going to change the height a tiny bit just to make it fill up the space a little bit better. We'll change that up. We don't have to worry about the treemap 'cause that automatically fits there. We just want to make sure that our bar chart is always a fit width and that's in case we switch to a different dimension or measure that may be pretty long. We don't want to squish the chart. Next up, we're just going to show our parameter. So I'm just going to click on any heat.
>
> **[14:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/sheet-selectors?u=76281980&t=848)** I'll go into our parameters, drop down, and it looks like it's already present. So this can happen, especially when you have your loading sheets in here. So you didn't see the parameter come out when you brought out the sheet, but it's somewhere on the dashboard. Now it can be a little difficult to find because we've got so many layered sheets here, but if you go to your layout, you can always find where those end up. And I typically find that they'll end up in this tiled section, the automatic tiled section that Tableau creates. And you just need to drill down until you start seeing parameters. Parameters look like this little line with a circle in it, like a slider, and you can see which parameters are back there. Now in this case, I might just end up floating this entire container, so I'm just going to right click on it and choose float. And that's got all of our parameters that we've created so far, and some legends as well. I'm just going to take out this legend because we don't need it right now, but we can see our parameter here that we created for our sheet selector. So I'm going to go ahead and choose treemap and you can just see how these visualizations are changing it out in the same sized area. This type of dynamic visual is really what we're looking for and what we're trying to accomplish with sheet selectors. We're trying to change out wholesale different visualizations. Like we mentioned, if you need something to change out a measure or dimension, you can use those multi metrics and multi-dimensions
>
> **[15:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/sheet-selectors?u=76281980&t=941)** and you should be pretty all set with that. It will keep the same visualization and change those pills out for you. But if you're changing out your visualization wholesale, then you're going to want to take the sheet selector route. Now I should mention that there are other methods to do sheet selectors. You can layer visualizations over each other in their entirety. That can cause some issues with tool tips and other things like that. I find that if you use sheet selectors and you keep your sheet selectors to under about four sheets, then the end user really shouldn't notice. Once you get over that, they might notice some shifting of the sheets just because of that one pixel buffer that you find inside the container. But for the most part, this is a really great way to change out your visuals and make your dashboard really dynamic. Next up, we'll be diving into filter menus.

### 3. Parameter and Set Actions

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Interactivity](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interactivity?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/interactivity?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Next up we're going to be talking about advanced interactivity. Interactivity grants users the ability to dynamically change visualizations, their analytic components and physical structure simply by selecting a visualization. Now, this can be accomplished in many ways, but parameter and set actions enable next level dashboard interaction for many types of use cases. We'll go through eight specific use cases leveraging both parameter actions and set actions. These will include multi-measures, index charts, year-over-year, sheet selectors, relative dimensional analysis, proportional brushing, asymmetric drill down, and color scaling. Now, a couple of these might sound familiar, being the multi-measure and the sheet selector, and yes, they are similar to what we've already created, but we're changing the type of interactivity that's going to be occurring. Rather than that dropdown, we're actually going to be changing that out for a selection. It's a subtle but important change that will change the interactivity of the dashboard and hopefully give the end user an aha moment. Now, many of the other use cases that we're going through are going to be for very specific business cases, but you'll be able to apply many of the topics that we're talking about to any industry or business use case. Up next, let's dive into how to create a parameter action.

#### [Parameter actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/parameter-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/parameter-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] So before we dive into our interactivity use cases that can leverage parameter actions, let's jump into what a parameter action is and what it does. Parameter actions allow end users to apply parameter values by selecting a visualization. These actions drive the flow of analysis in an intuitive manner without the standard dropdown box functionality that you find with regular parameter actions. The dropdown still exist, but we're not leveraging them on the dashboard. We're actually using the visualizations as the buttons to change the parameters. Now, there's a couple steps in creating parameter actions, but they're very similar to the normal actions that you use every day in filter actions. First, you'll create the parameter action. It's done through the same menu that the other actions are done from. Go to dashboard, go to actions, and you'll choose the change parameter option. Just a quick note, unlike filter actions, you won't be able to create a parameter action straight from the top-right hand corner of your sheet that is specifically left only for filter actions. Parameter actions have to be done through this menu. The settings that you can customize are also very similar to your filter actions. You can modify the name, you can choose the source sheets that the parameter will come from. You can choose the run action on, which is how the action will occur. Does it occur when you hover, when you select via a menu inside a tool tip. There is a slight difference
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/parameter-actions?u=76281980&t=94)** since these are parameters and not actions that are going to filter a specific category, you're going to choose your target parameter, so which parameter you want impacted by the value that you're pushing to it. And then for the value section, you're going to choose which field is going to push into that parameter. So this is obviously going to be a field that exists in the sheet. It will be picked up from what you select on, and then it will be pushed into the parameter. Now, in some cases, you'll want this field to aggregate, but in most cases, you probably won't. You'll leave this as a default as none. But if you are using it in specific manner, you may choose a min or a max or something like that to get the value that you want. So next up, we're going to dive straight into use cases, leveraging parameter actions, and the first will be to impact the multi-measures that we've already created.

Again, multi-measures allow users to dynamically select metrics in a dashboard. Enabling parameter actions as the driver rather than a dropdown, will create an intuitive and immersive experience for our end users. Rather than using a dropdown, they're going to actually select the visualization and push that value into the parameter. Anything connected to that parameter will update automatically and change the visualization as such. Now this three main steps to leveraging parameter actions for multi-measures, first we'll create a calculated field. Now this calculated field is just going to be a shell that we're going to push from our visualizations. Now obviously we can use parameter actions to push into our parameters from any sheet within a dashboard and we can use the dimensions that are available in that sheet to do that, but in this case, we're leveraging calculated fields that hold the value that we want to push to the parameter and we're going to layer those calculated fields into the source sheet so they just act as a vessel to move that value into the parameter. #### [Parameter actions: Index chart](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3956017?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3956017?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Alright, so next up, we're going to be leveraging parameter actions to create an index chart in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Index charts allow for dynamic relative time comparison along an axis. Essentially with the use of parameter actions, once a date is selected, all other points will change relative to that date. Now index charts are used across various industries. They're very common when looking at the stock market, commodity markets, and when looking at seasonality of markets. Index charts have particular value because they're looking at the relative change of values rather than the raw values in the trend. This is another important way that analysts look at their data, especially over time. By leveraging parameter actions to accomplish our index chart, we can have the user dynamically choose a particular point in time and change the index chart at will. Now, there's a few different pieces that we need to create to make this work in Tableau, but it's relatively straightforward. We're going to need a multi-metric table calculation. This metric will use the difference from tablecalc while also referencing the index date that you've chosen by selecting the visualization. We'll also leverage a multi-metric. The multi-metric will be used as a reference point. It will be dual-axised against our dynamic index chart, so we can always keep a pulse on what the actual values are. The index date is the date that will be pushed into the parameter and the color legend will give us some clarity as far as how far we are off from the index date's value, either positive or negative.

> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3956017?u=76281980&t=469)** We want to choose index date. The chart will now be relative to our particular index date's value, no matter what. So you can see how this makes this much more intuitive for an end user to select. You're not choosing from a dropdown. You're not hovering over, saying, "Ooh, August 19th would be nice," then going to a dropdown, choosing August 19th, and then going back. You can just click on exactly what you want to see and the visualization will change to represent that. #### [Parameter actions: Relative dimensional analysis](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Up next, our last parameter action use case is relative dimensional analysis. Relative dimensional analysis gives users the ability to compare a single dimension in context to or relative to all other dimensional values within a selected field. Now that's really just a fancy way of saying if I click on a dimension, I want to see all other dimensions relative value in context to this dimension. And this might sound a little bit familiar, this is actually pretty much the same thing that we were doing with our index chart. We're clicking on a specific date and it was indexing all the other dates to that specific date and comparing the values between those two. Now with relative dimensional analysis, we're choosing other dimensions. They're not dates, but they are different dimensions that we want to compare against. So we're doing that selection here, picking a particular dimension, looking at the measure value, and doing a comparison against the other measure values of the dimensions within the view. Once we select that dimension, you'll see all of the other dimensions change. We can do this with color and labels to give the end user a better idea of what's happening. Again, this use case is useful in the real world, especially in situations where you're looking at individual performance of a particular dimension, but you also want to benchmark the entire set of dimensions off a particular selection. Now, as far as creating this relative dimensional analysis in our sheet, it's relatively straightforward. We're going to go ahead and just create
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=94)** our dimension parameter, and that's just going to pick our dimensions out from a field existing in the dashboard. Then we're going to leverage a parameter action, which is going to drive the parameter selection that I've chosen from the view, push it into the parameter that we're looking at, and then it's going to impact a calculation where we're isolating that dimension and pulling up that value. Once that value's isolated, it can be compared against to all the other dimensional values in the view. In our view in particular, we're going to be showing this vibrantly in the color scale, and this will give the end user an immediate point of feedback based on what they select on. Now that we have a high level overview of what relative dimensional analysis is and how we do it, let's go ahead and go into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) desktop and create the relative dimensional analysis calculations and parameter actions in our workbook. Alright, so we're back in our workbook. The first thing we need to do is use our alt key and open up our filter menu because we need to change out our view to look at our tree map. That's where we're going to apply our relative dimensional analysis. So we're going to have to go to bars, go to tree map and swap that out. And then we can close our filter menu for now and we won't need to open that up for a little bit. And then we can just click on our sheet and go to it. Now we could have found the sheet just on our bottom section, but it would've been blank and we would've had to change that parameter anyway, so that's why we went in there and did that. Next up, we're going to create our parameter.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=187)** So let's just right click in the white space, say create parameter, and we're going to call this relative dimensional analysis selection. Sounds pretty fancy, I kind of like it. We're going to go ahead and actually change this to be a string. We're going to go and choose list. Now, instead of adding our list in manually, we're going to actually add a list from one of our fields contained in the data source. All right, so next up we're going to go in and choose fixed and add values from. We're going to go over and select Maven Roasters, and we're going to actually add in three different fields worth of data. The reason for this is we're leveraging a multi-dimension in this view, and we need to have all the dimensions available in this parameter. It doesn't really matter how it looks in this parameter. No one's actually going to go in and select from a list here, but we need all the values available for our parameter action to pick it up. So let's go ahead and choose product group first, and then we can add in even more values. We're going to go down and choose product category, and you can see they're a pending here, so that's good. We're going to add another, which is just product. So we've got all of them in here. This is looking good. We're going to hit okay, and that will save our parameter out there. All right, so next up we're going to create our calculated field. So let's just right click and choose create calculated field. So now we're going to name this calculation selected dimension, year over year diff percent, and I'm going to actually paste the calculation in just
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=281)** so I can walk you through it. Let's go ahead and put that in there and I'll walk you step by step through what this is doing. You can see that we're creating a level of detail calculation. And while we did go over level details in the beginner course, we're going to get much more into them in our advanced calculation section of this course, but we are going to need one for this particular use case. So let's walk through exactly what this is doing. Let's look at the inside first. It's the easiest way to read our calculated fields. So we're essentially saying that if the multi-dimension, which is that multi-dimension field we created equals the value placed in the parameter that we just made, the relative dimensional analysis selection, then we are going to pull in the multi measure year over year difference percent, specifically, we're going to pull that measure in for the multi-dimension shown here. So this entire top section is doing a fixed level detail calculation just to get the value of the specified dimension that the parameter is showing. So it's going to bring in this particular measure, which is the multi measure year over year difference percent for the specific parameter dimension that we're selecting. It's going to take that and it's going to subtract the regular multi measure year over year difference percent. So that means if I was hovering over beverages, the multi measure year over year difference percent would be for beverages.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=374)** That's what this number would be. But this number up top, this one that I'm subtracting by, that one is going to be related to only the one that I have inside this parameter and that parameter is going to be generated based on what I'm selecting. So if I went over and selected merchandise, this top section would be the multi measure year over year difference for merchandise. And then if I looked at multi measure year over year difference here, that would be for whatever I'm hovering over. So if I hovered over food, it would take merchandise minus food and it would gimme that value. So part of this is dynamic and it will change based on what you're hovering over and part of it is fixed. That's why we're using a fixed LOD to exclude all the other dimensions and just concentrate on the one that we've created and that will end up being a very powerful calculation for us. So feel free to copy this down into your calculated field. And then once you're done, hit okay and we're going to apply this measure. What this is really going to show us is how much better or worse a dimension is doing versus the selected dimension. So if I click on a particular dimension and all the other boxes are bright orange or dark gray, I'll be able to know all of these other dimensions are doing much better than the one that I've selected or they're doing much worse. And that will give me some key points of information, some comparative analysis inside the stream app that will help me make my business decisions.
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=468)** Now, to get this to actually apply, all we're going to need to do is drag our brand new calculated field onto color, drop that in, and then we're going to modify our color scheme. Let's just double click on our legend. We're going to go through and we're going to choose the gold, purple diverging option. We're going to make sure that this isn't set to reverse. We're going to change our purple or to our very nice 55555 option for a hex code. And we're going to hit apply. So that looks just like we had it before, but it's going to be able to dynamically change based on our parameter action once we put that in. So in general, when you're looking at this, anything in this gold color will be worse than our selected dimension, and anything in this gray color will be better than our selected dimension in terms of year over year difference percent. Alright, so let's go back into our dashboard and we're going to go ahead and apply our parameter action. So let's go down to our dashboard menu, choose actions. We're going to say add action, change parameter. We're going to rename this be multi-dimension selector, and then we're going to highlight everything with a shift, uncheck them all, and we're going to choose our source sheet. Now our source sheet's going to be this tree map, so we're going to select there. We want this action to happen upon selecting. So we'll leave select chosen. We're going to pick our target parameter, which is going to be our relative dimensional analysis selection. We'll click on that.
>
> **[9:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955027?u=76281980&t=563)** Then we're going to choose which field is going to push into here. So we're going to go ahead and choose our multi-dimension Maven Roasters, and our aggregation will be none because we're using a dimension. Let's go ahead and click okay and click okay again. And now we can click on any dimension or visualization. And the should change based on the relative dimensional analysis. So let's click on beverages. Alright, look at that. Our color changes. So whole beans and teas is doing worse than beverages, but food is doing better. Let's click on food. Yep, that makes sense. Beverages is doing worse, these are doing worse. And add-ons are doing a little better. Let's click on add-ons is one more check. Okay, looking good. These guys are doing a little better than add-ons. These are doing a little worse. So again, this can be a really cool dynamic tool for our end users to leverage. They can at will just click on the visualizations and do that comparative dimensional analysis on the fly. #### [Set actions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/set-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/set-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Next up, we're going to go through our set actions use cases. Now, set actions allow end users to change set values by simply selecting a visualization. Set actions provide a distinct advantage over traditional sets. While normal sets allow users to group dimensions together for filtering and analysis later, set actions allow sets to be modified on the fly and then retained or released by the user. As a result, sets can be combined with calculations and filters in a much more dynamic way. Set actions can actually be used to modify calculations, filters, reference lines, [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) queries, and visualizations. On the fly analysis with set actions is a huge asset to our end users as they interact with their visualizations. To leverage set actions, you have to follow two specific steps. First, create your set action. Now set actions are found in the same actions menu as our parameter actions and our filter actions. You'll just go to dashboard, actions, change set values. Note that set actions can't be created in the same way that filter actions can directly from the sheet. Now, once your set action's created, you need to customize your settings, that can include adjusting the name, choosing your source sheets, where you'll select where your action will be generated from, choosing your run action on, so hover, select, or menu, choosing a target set.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/set-actions?u=76281980&t=92)** So you need to have a set created first that your set action will be populating. You'll pick which set that gets populated down in your target set section and then you'll modify your running action will and your clearing selection will options. Now, your running action will option essentially says what is going to happen when I enable the set action. You can assign values to the set, you can add values to the set or you can remove values from the set. On the other side of the coin, you can also set up your clearing selection options and this includes keeping the values, adding all values to the set and then removing all values from your set. Much alike parameter actions, there are specific use cases that work best with set actions, we're going to be going through a few high level key ones, but there's really endless possibilities to leveraging both parameter and set actions for your visualizations. Now that we've gone through set actions high level, in the next lesson we're going to be diving straight into our first use case, which is going to be related to proportional brushing.

#### [Set actions: Asymmetric drilldown](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3953017?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3953017?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so our next set action use case is going to be focused on asymmetric drilldown. Asymmetric drilldown provides hierarchy drilldown for a selected dimension while leaving other dimensions compressed. With normal [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) hierarchy functionality, you'll click on a product group, as in this example, and it will show all product groups open up for all product detail, which will be your next sub dimension to open up. But with asymmetric drilldown driven by set actions, you'll be able to select a specific dimension and open up a specific dimensions sub dimension values. You can see here that when we select on food, we're only going to get the product detail for food and not everything else contained within the hierarchy. Asymmetric drilldown is built via two steps. The first step is to create your set and your set action. The set is created from the higher level hierarchy dimension, which is subsequently pushed by the set action when selected. The second step is to create a calculation. The calculation will default to a blank value when the set is not engaged and by default, the set action will return to empty. Now this is all accomplished through a very simple if statement. Inside the if statement, the set is really just a Boolean clause. When it's activated, it will say that it is true. When it is true, then it will reveal the sub-dimension,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3953017?u=76281980&t=94)** otherwise it will stay blank. Now that we've talked high level about asymmetric drilldown, let's go ahead and create our own asymmetric drilldown sheet inside Tableau desktop. All right, so we're inside Tableau desktop. We're going to create a new sheet to start. Let's go ahead and right click on info icon, choose new worksheet, and we're just going to double click and call this Asymmetric Drilldown. First up, we're going to create a set. So let's just look up the field product group. We'll right click on product group, create a set, and that set is going to be called Asymmetric Set. And we're just going to select all values for now and choose okay. So if we look up set, we now have Asymmetric Set available. Next up we're going to create our calculated field. That'll just give the end user the idea that they need to click something for something to happen. We're going to add the calculation, which states if the asymmetric set, then product else blank. And then we'll do end at the end of the if statement. So again, the asymmetric set when activated will state true and then we'll be able to show our product. Otherwise, it's just going to leave it blank. Let's hit okay on that and our calculated field is all set.

>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3953017?u=76281980&t=281)** We're going to choose our Maven_Roasters data source. Our set will be our Asymmetric Set, Asymmetric Set Action we'll call it. Then we're going to assign our values, so we're going to say assign values to set, and then for clearing our selection, we're going to actually say remove all values from set. So that will take everything out when we're deselected. Let's hit okay and okay again and try this out. Let's go ahead and just click on add-ons, and there we go. We've just got the product detail for add-ons and we'll click again and it will shrink back down. So you can see how that works now. When we click on product group, product group for add-ons gets added to the set. Because it's added to the set, we're able to show the product detail. When we click on it again, it will compress. We'll keep our column here, but it will blank out and aggregate our rows up so that we can see just our high level information. All right, that's looking great. Next up, we're going to dive into color scaling with set actions.

#### [Set actions: Color scaling](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955026?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955026?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So the final set action use case is going to be related to color scaling. Color scaling enables cross dimensional comparison of subsets of data, which are normally obscured by outliers within the dataset, i.e., very large or very small values, which impact our scale. When looking at this in a visualization, it essentially means that if you had a measure applied to a color scale, you may have a very dark or a very light value and that value may be so large or so small that it drastically impacts the other values in the dataset, and you really don't get a nice scale or range for the dimensions that you're looking at. What color scaling does is it allows you to choose a specific subset of data and then have the color scaling applied to just that subset. In practicality, this will look more like this visualization where you're selecting a certain subset of this treemap and it's rescaling based on that specific set. Set actions are particularly good at applying color scaling because they can group those dimensions into a particular set, and then the measures can be applied to the color scale and we can have a reset of that color scaling for our specific dimensions. Now, to create color scaling, first we need to create a set. The base set will be created off the dimensions that we're looking to color scale against. Our calculated field will only show the metric when our dimensions are enabled, meaning our set action will push our dimensions into this calculation and only reveal values for the selected dimensions.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3955026?u=76281980&t=95)** And finally, our set action will push the selected dimensions into that calculated field. This calculated field will be applied to the color within the visualization, so that we can get a nice color scaling for our treemap. Now let's go ahead and jump in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop and apply color scaling in our workbook. Okay, so we're back in Tableau Desktop and we're on our main workbook. First up, we're going to go ahead and use our alt key and just open up our filter menu. We're going to swap out our filter menu to look at the treemap. So once that's chosen, we're going to go in and change our dimension. #### [Pro tip: Viz animations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-animations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-animations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For our final section of the Dynamic Design section of this course, we're going to go through a quick pro tip around Viz Animations. Viz Animations provide a dynamic transition setting for visualizations using filters, filter actions, calculations, and more. Now, prior to Viz Animations, when you would change visualization via a filter or any other mechanism, the visualization would change quickly, but there wouldn't be any sort of transition period. The visualization would blank out momentarily and then display the new values. Now, what Viz Animations do is it shows a progression of the change from your original value to the new value that you've selected. So when you go ahead and change that value, it'll look more like this, where you have your original, and then as you progress through the data to your new selection, you can see your fields dynamically change. It's definitely a nice to have, but it can absolutely retain your users for longer, and it can inspire them to dig deeper into your dashboard. Now there are a few settings that you can change inside your Viz Animations panel. Viz Animations by default, are set to off, so they do impact performance slightly. That's why they're set to off by default inside a workbook. But to turn them on, you can either do that at the workbook level, or at the selected sheet level. And you can customize each sheet individually if you don't want animations to apply, or if you want a certain setting for a certain type of visualization. You can control the duration of your animations. You can make them be fast,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-animations?u=76281980&t=93)** which is around 0.3 seconds per transition, or you can set them to slow, which is two seconds. Or you can even pick a custom value in between. The style of Viz Animations is either simultaneous, which means all the Viz Animations will happen at once, or sequential, meaning that each visualization will go in order and change one at a time. And as mentioned, you can also change all of those default options at a sheet level. So if you want a particular sheet to not have an animation, or only one of your sheets to have an animation on a dashboard, you can certainly do that as well. Let's go ahead and apply Viz Animations to our own dashboard inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop. All right, so we're back in Tableau Desktop. Now, to modify our animations, we can just go to the Format Menu and choose Animations. From this menu, we can turn animations on for the entire workbook, and we can modify the animations per sheet. Let's go ahead and choose on, and this will turn on our animations for our entire workbook. Now we're going to customize our animations a little bit. We want to actually keep the animation duration fast. We don't want this to go too slow, but we do want to change the style. So instead of simultaneous, we want to use sequential. We have a bunch of different visualizations in our view, and we don't want these to confuse the end user by changing all at once. So we're going to have it in a sequential order. Now, let's go ahead and go down to our index chart, and let's just change out our index date. But instead of doing this normally,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-animations?u=76281980&t=187)** now we have animations enacted. So let's see what that looks like. We're going to go ahead and just choose a particular date. And you'll see our animations transition the data. Now, that might have been a little too fast to catch, so if we slow that down for you a little bit, maybe you can see it a little better. Let's go ahead and choose slow. So this'll make the transition take a little bit more time. We'll click slow, and then we'll change our animation. See that? So it's changing a little bit, and it's moving our bars up and down and you can see them sort of grow and shrink. So let's set our workbook default to fast, but let's change and keep our index date at a slower, one-second changeover. 'Cause when we tested this out, you know it was a little too fast. You couldn't really tell there was an animation there, and this is where you can actually get into customizing at a Viz level. So our Viz Animations are ready to go. We can customize these as we go along, but it's as simple as that to activate these really, really nice custom Viz Animations inside your workbook. Up next, we'll be diving into the homework use case for the Dynamic Design section of the course.

#### [Pro tip: Viz extensions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-extensions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-extensions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Okay, so now that we looked at viz animations, let's check out one more pro tip around custom visualizations. Viz extensions are web applications that can extend the native visual capabilities of [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Viz extensions give users the ability to interact with custom viz types on their worksheets. Tableau extensions expand worksheet functionality with the help of web applications created by Tableau, Tableau partners, and third party developers within our community. Adding viz extensions to your workbook is straightforward. Go to a sheet and locate the marks card visualization dropdown. From that menu, go down to viz extension and then click add extension. The viz extension dialogue box will pop-up with viz extension options from various third party vendors as well as those developed by Tableau. Choose the viz extension you'd like to use from the menu and select open. You may be prompted with a data disclosure at this time. Be cautious when using viz extensions with sensitive or proprietary data. Viz extensions leverage aggregate-level data to create visualizations and may pass data securely to third party developers. Ask your company security or IT team for approval before using viz extensions with real data. Once this piece is done, all that's left is to configure your viz. Settings and functionality will differ depending on which viz extension you're using. What will be consistent is that these functions will be found within the Marks Card. I'd recommend testing out
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-viz-extensions?u=76281980&t=93)** the Tableau-authored viz extensions first as you dip your toes into the world of extensions, as they're the most robust and well-tested extensions in the library. Now that we've talked high level about viz extensions, let's jump into Tableau Desktop and create a test viz extension in our workbook. We won't use this visual for our final dashboard, but we will test viz extensions to ensure you know how to properly add them and configure them in Tableau Desktop. Okay, so we're back in Tableau Desktop, and we're going to create a brand new sheet called viz extension test. Feel free to test this out with a bunch of different options. You can go ahead and change the order of your sankey just by moving your marks around on your marks card. So feel free to play with it. You won't be able to break anything. In worst case scenario, you just start over again and try it. But as you can see, it's much, much easier to create these custom visualizations than it used to be inside Tableau. No special data padding needed, just loading up your extension and then using your standard dimensions and measures.

### 4. Geospatial Mapping

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Geospatial mapping](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-mapping?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-mapping?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Up next, we'll be diving into the Geospatial Mapping section of this course. Geospatial mapping includes any geographic visualization or calculation leveraged by [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). We'll cover geospatial mapping in the context of Data & Workspace, Use-Cases, and Customization. The Data & Workspace lessons will cover ingesting geospatial data and exploring the geospatial tools in Tableau Desktop. Geospatial Use-Cases will explore various business scenarios and use cases for geospatial mapping. Customization will cover customizing maps and expanding on simple visuals using internal and external tools. Geospatial mapping is one of the most important and powerful tools that Tableau provides. There are many ways that geospatial data can be applied and integrated into your analysis, and we won't be covering all of those use cases, but we'll be covering many of the most integral pieces of geospatial mapping that you can leverage inside Tableau Desktop. First up, we'll dive into exploring geospatial data.

#### [Geospatial data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So the first piece of geospatial mapping that you'll need to understand is geospatial data. Geospatial data comes in two primary [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md) in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop, geographic fields, and spatial data files. Most users are first introduced to geospatial fields as part of mapping. Spatial files typically come later in development maturity, though both aspects of geospatial data are relatively straightforward and easy to use. A geographic role can be assigned to fields in any source, so long as those field types and roles are correctly assigned. Most geospatial fields will auto assign as long as the names are close to their geospatial counterparts. For example, fields called state, city, and ZIP will automatically become geographic role fields. If they don't, you can assign them just by right clicking on the field, going to geographic role, and selecting the appropriate role. An important tip around geographic fields is that most are string type dimensions. The loan exception is latitude and longitude, which are coded as number decimals. If you can't assign a geographic role, it may be an indication that your field is not a string type, or if you're looking at latitude and longitude, those should be coded as number decimals to be able to accept their geographic role. Spatial data files are special file types that contain geospatial data using geometry fields. Examples include Shape files, GeoJSON files, and more. Spatial data files are typically produced
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980&t=96)** by proprietary tools like Alteryx or Esri. Spatial files can display very complex geospatial visuals, and as such, can add tremendous depth and grain to the spatial analysis in your [Dashboards](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Dashboards.md). A quick pro tip around geospatial data is that latitude and longitude fields are automatically generated with standard location fields. As long as the location fields have a geographic role, they'll generate a latitude and longitude field. You'll notice that these fields are generated because they'll say generated in parentheses after the name latitude or longitude. The latitude and longitude that are generated will focus around the center point of whatever you're visualizing. So if you're visualizing a state or a city, it'll pick the center point within that state or city. If you need exact latitude and longitude, it needs to be imported into Tableau from the data source. There are many open source and proprietary tools that can geocode your data, but you won't need proprietary tools for everything down to the voting block level. Tableau provides a very robust geospatial environment for you to visualize. Let's go ahead and jump into Tableau Desktop and explore geographic fields and spatial data files. So we're back in Tableau Desktop. Let's go ahead and go to our BAN sales tab, and let's release any searches that we had on our main data source. First up, we're going to explore some geographic fields. Let's go ahead and search for store postal code.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980&t=190)** We're just going to search for store, and we'll go down to store postal code. Now, the first thing we notice about store postal code is it's not a geographic field. A geographic field would be one like the store state province, or store city, because we have the geographic indicator on the left hand side. If we click on the indicator and go down to geographic role, we can see that the store state province is set to state or province. We can also see that the store city is set to city. These are the appropriate geographic roles for these geographic fields. But we know that store postal code should be coded to a ZIP code geographic role. The name is slightly off from ZIP code, and we also have the wrong field type for this field. As we mentioned prior, almost all geographic fields should be encoded as string. The lone exception are latitude and longitude fields. This field is coded as a number (whole). We're going to want to go ahead and choose string, and then we're going to go ahead and change our geographic role. So again, we'll click on the A, B, C here, we'll go down to geographic role, and we'll set it to ZIP code/postal code. Let's create a new sheet and test this new field out. We're going to name this sheet test map. Let's go ahead and just click on store lookup count and Control + Click on store postal code and create our first map. We'll go over to our symbol maps under show me and just select. And we can see here that we're generating a map
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980&t=283)** at the ZIP code level, leveraging our store postal code field only, and we're generating a latitude and longitude that's placing a mark at the average location in the center of that ZIP code. This is a fast, easy, and accurate way of generating maps inside Tableau. Next up, let's connect to a spatial data file. We'll go over to our new data source window, and we're going to go down and choose spatial data file. Now, in your data pack, you should have a file called Maven_Roasters_Competitor_Location.shp. This is a Shape file, also known as a spatial data file. Let's go ahead and connect to our spatial data file. Now, it should be noted that you should have your other spatial referential files in the same location as your main Shape file. In our case, we're going to have our other DBFPRG and SHX files in the same location as our shape file so that it can be read accurately. Let's go ahead and click on our Shape file and hit open, and we'll get our data pane. Now we're going to go ahead and just ingest this data directly. So let's just go ahead and click on test map and that will extract our data out. You'll see that we have our new Maven_Roasters_Competitor_Locations data source available. We're going to go ahead and create a new sheet. This sheet will be called "New York Competitors." Then we'll go over, deselect our search, and all we need to do is bring out our geometry field. Our dimensions can be layered in for extra detail,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-data?u=76281980&t=378)** and we can also leverage our latitude and longitude fields independently. But since we have our geometry field, and that is a geospatial field, we can just double click on that and it will automatically generate our map. Now, by default, when you bring out a geometry field, it's going to render everything as technically one mark. So if I highlight, you'll see everything lights up. If I want to bring in any other granularity and have individual points, I can bring in the grain from my dimensions. So let's say I bring in my store ID and drop that on my detail shelf. #### [The mapping workspace](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/the-mapping-workspace?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/the-mapping-workspace?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Geospatial mapping in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is unique that it gets its own mapping workspace. "The mapping workspace provides an array of customization and feature enablement tools for geospatial visualizations in Tableau." The mapping workspace includes panes, menus, and tabs, where geospatial information can be customized. Note that many menus cannot be accessed unless a map is visualized in the view. This includes your map legend, map layers, map options, and more. Let's take a quick look at all the different options provided by the mapping workspace. Now, the mapping workspace lives in your traditional sheet workspace, but many options and tools will appear when you visualize geospatial data. On the left hand side, you'll see your data pane fields, geographic fields show in the data pane with a globe icon. Inside your marks card, you can adjust the map type from the mark dropdown or drag geographic fields to detail to impact the grain of the map. The view toolbar will enable map searching, panning, zooming, and multiple mark selection. Your map options will control options available to the end users on the map, related to exploration, scale and units. You can customize what you want to show to the end users and what you'd like to hide. The map menu provides additional map capability, including background images and map layers. The map layers pane will cover the data and analytics pane
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/the-mapping-workspace?u=76281980&t=94)** when it's revealed, much like the formatting pane does when you format any piece of your dashboard. Map layers will help you control the coloration, level of geographic detail, and integrated data layers presented in your view. Map layers will be your primary menu to customize the look and feel of your map. Now that we've gone through the mapping workspace at a high level, let's dive into Tableau Desktop and apply some of these features and functionality inside our own workbook. But as we get into more detail, we can also add things like coastline, terrain, and many more. Some options will be grayed out, because you're zoomed out too far to be able to see them. If you zoom in a little bit, you'll be able to add your zip code boundaries, neighborhoods, and building footprints. >
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-use-cases?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Geospatial mapping is an extremely powerful tool. Maps are used for their ability to answer spatial questions, and geospatial mapping can help us do that in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). That being said, there are several common use cases that analysts can use to explore and visualize geospatial data in Tableau, including heat maps, density maps, point distribution charts, and more. We're going to cover six key geospatial use cases in this section. While six use cases doesn't cover them all, what we'll go through will get you off on the right foot to leveraging geospatial mapping in tableau. A quick note before we talk through the geospatial use cases at a high level is that you shouldn't create maps just for the sake of creating maps. In some cases, a bar chart will answer your question quicker and more effectively. Maps are an extremely powerful tool, but shouldn't be used as a one size fits all analytics option. Point distribution maps can answer questions like which cities have our highest sales and which are most profitable? Do we have any outliers based on geographic location? Heat maps can answer questions like I need a quick visual showing our highest impact marketing campaigns by state. Density maps can help you look into which streets and intersections have the highest occurrences of accidents in your city. Flow maps can answer questions like, what's the optimal route for our truck drivers?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/geospatial-use-cases?u=76281980&t=93)** Which flights give the most direct route for our international shipments? Layered maps can help you analyze questions like, what is the demographic makeup near my proposed store locations? Does city and state level performance differ significantly, or is it pretty much the same? And finally, radius maps can help answer questions like, where are my closest competitors? How far do our employees commute to the [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md), and which qualify for travel compensation? Obviously, there are many more potential applications for geospatial mapping, but we'll cover these six potential use cases as we work through the geospatial mapping section of this course. Keep an eye out for these icons in the bottom right hand of each slide as we work through this section. These will identify which geospatial use cases we're looking at as we develop our visualizations.

#### [Standard maps](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/standard-maps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/standard-maps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's jump into geospatial mapping with our standard [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) maps. There are two types of standard maps available in Tableau, proportional symbol and filled maps. Proportional symbol maps are ideal for representing points on a map and blending multiple measures into your visualization. Symbol maps leverage symbols placed at latitude and longitude coordinates based on geographic fields used in the view. Now, these fields don't have to be exactly latitude and longitude fields. They can be any geospatial field in your dataset, including country, state, city, zip, and more. Note that proportional symbol maps are the only map type that can cleanly be used at a city level. As cities don't typically have standard boundaries, and as a result filled maps can't be leveraged at city level. Proportional symbol maps come in especially handy if you're dealing with geospatial information that varies in size widely. One of my favorite examples coming from the home state of Rhode Island in the United States is that my state was so small you could never really see it on a map, especially when you are zoomed out to the entire United States. A proportional symbol map would've placed the same size circle on each state if I were to represent it that way in Tableau. In this way, it normalizes the symbols across your map to give everything an even look and feel. Now you can modify the size of the symbol being represented using a measure,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/standard-maps?u=76281980&t=94)** but you'll still get a much more even distribution across your map. Field maps are better for use cases with heat map visuals and leveraging the size and shape of our geographic areas. Field maps leverage polygons to produce shaded area maps. Most standard geographic and geometric fields can be used to produce field maps with the exception of city level. Field maps are extremely common and often paired with heat maps. One common example of field maps are political maps. You'll often see filled heat map style maps down to the polling place level. As we work through some examples of standard maps in Tableau Desktop, we'll be applying three of our use cases, including point distribution maps, heat maps, and density maps. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/custom-territories?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Next up, let's jump into custom territories. Custom territories allow users to dynamically select or consolidate geographic areas using traditional [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) grouping functionality. Custom territories can be very useful for creating non-standard geographic groupings. To create a custom territory, you simply enable a regular group function on a geographic field. In the example shown here, we're creating a group using our zip field, so we've bundled various zip codes into boroughs in New York City. Then all we have to do is bring out our new geographic group into our level of detail and remove any level of detail that's geographic and lower in grain than our geographic group. This will allow our field map to combine areas that were normally represented with that lower grain geographic field. In the example shown, instead of showing individual zip codes, those zip codes are grouped into larger groups that we've created using our geographic grouping field. The reason that we remove the lower grain geographic fields is so that we get these solid geographic groupings in the view and they're not split up by those lower grain fields. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/map-hierarchies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Mapping hierarchies allow users to drill into custom geographic levels of detail within a map. Map hierarchies are similar to dimensional hierarchies in that they allow for drill down functionality. But map hierarchies specifically drill through geographic enabled fields, which will dynamically change the visualization and what level of detail on the map is displayed. You can create map hierarchies through three simple steps. First, you'll create your standard hierarchy by right clicking on your dimension field, going to Hierarchy and choosing Create Hierarchy. After the hierarchy has been created, you can drag in your fields that you want to add to the hierarchy. Once the hierarchy is created, you'll add that to your view and you'll be able to drill up and down just like you would with any other hierarchy. The main difference being the grain that you're drilling into will dynamically change the mapping view to either [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in and show more detail or zoom out and show less detail. Let's jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop and create mapping hierarchies of our own. Advanced formatting tools and third party maps enable a deep level of customization within [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) desktop.
