#### [Map layers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/map-layers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/map-layers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about customizing maps with map layers. Map layers provide a space for developers to customize the standard map layers inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). These can impact all areas of the map, including color, lines, background style, and data layers, including layers like demographics. Let's break down the three main parts of map layers. If we take a look at our Map Layers menu, you can see at the very top, we have a section called Background. The background represents the overarching styling of our background map. The default map box and open street map styles are built into Tableau and don't require any extra integration. Note that offline maps are also available in the main map menu as well. Our next section is called Map Layers. This is the bulk of the options that you have to customize. Map layers includes dimensional layers that can be revealed or hidden by the developer. These can include things like land cover, coastline, zip code boundaries, and more. Note that some of the options shown won't be visible to users until they [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) into the map. So things like zip code or county names might not be visible until you zoom in, and then they will turn from gray to black inside the Map Layers menu. The best thing to do with customizing map layers is honestly just to try them out. There's no harm in selecting or deselecting any of these options
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/map-layers?u=76281980&t=93)** to test out if these layers are options for your analysis. The key question is, do they add insight to your analytics, or are they just making your maps look busy? You want to strike a good balance between design and functionality using your map layers. In addition to your background and your main Map Layers menu, you also have the option to layer data in. The data layer provides additional US data layers that can be displayed behind the data sets and metrics contained in your dashboard. This is mainly census level data that can display things like population, number of US households, housing demographics, and US population by race. Now there's even more ways to integrate other data layers into our maps, but these come standard with Tableau and don't require any extra effort to enable. Let's take a look at customizing our map layers inside Tableau Desktop. All right, so we're back in Tableau Desktop, and we're going to navigate to the Origin-Destination map tab. Now this map is pretty plain, so let's go ahead and customize this using our map layers. We're going to go up to our Map dropdown and we're going to select Map Layers. You'll see over on the left hand side, we have our standard Map Layers menu open and ready for us to customize. Inside the Style menu, we can change our background map to anything we want. Now it really depends on the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of our dashboard and how everything should look and feel,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/map-layers?u=76281980&t=187)** but in this case, we're going to leave this map as light. We're just going to add some different layer pieces and change things up a bit. Now, if you're interested in what Washout does, let's do a quick demo of that. Essentially what this will do, is it'll wash out our background map. Now, you might be thinking that a normal washout doesn't really make a whole lot of sense. Why would you want to see less of your background? Well, this can be particularly useful, let's say if you're creating a map of the United States, you've got state data, which is rendering all of the state's shapes, but you don't want to show any of the rest of the world, any coastline, or anything like that. You just want those state shapes to render in negative space. You can go ahead and wash out the remainder of your dashboard and just have your state shapes floating. All right, so let's go into our Map Layers and customize our map a bit. First up, we're going to add in the coastline just to give some better definition along our coasts in New York City. We're going to add streets and highways, just so people can get a baseline for where they are on the map. This is especially useful when we're zooming into big cities, where the street level view can be important. Let's go ahead and add in neighborhoods to add another extra level of grain. ### 5. Advanced Calculations

#### [Advanced calculations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/advanced-calculations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/advanced-calculations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so the next section of the course will be focused around advanced calculations. Advanced calculations go beyond basic [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) functions to help solve specific complex use cases. Advanced calculations will be broken down into three separate areas in this course. The first is regular expressions. Regular expressions, or RegEx, leverage text-based search pattern [Algorithms](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Algorithms.md) to find and replace string values. RegEx is particularly useful for text heavy data, and in the advent of [Big Data](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Big%20Data.md), there's more than enough use cases for RegEx to be applicable for your business. Our next focus will be table calculations. TableCalcs allow you to create custom calculations that exist within the scope of a specific visual. We learned the fundamentals of TableCalcs in our beginner course, but in this course we're going to apply TableCalcs for specific business use cases. And finally, we'll look at level of detail expressions. LODs allow users to dynamically control the grain of their analysis. We'll be applying LODs in new and interesting ways to solve very specific business use cases. On the whole, advanced calculations will be a practical application of our regular Tableau calculations in interesting and complex ways that will solve real world business use cases. In our next lesson, we're going to dive straight in to regular expressions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** find (1)

#### [Regular expressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Regular expressions were originally developed in the 1950s and '60s to use special characters to find text patterns. These patterns are used as a method of text processing, which was originally done on very early computers. RegEx is still used today in many different practices, and [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) has integrated regular expressions to have their own functions inside the calculation window. Let's do a quick walkthrough of the anatomy of a RegEx calculation inside Tableau. Here's an example RegEx expression. Let's break this down to its simplest terms. First, we have the RegEx function. This is a Tableau regular expression function used to call a specific action. Now in Tableau, there's four specific actions you can call, REPLACE, MATCH, EXTRACT, and EXTRACT_NTH. Each of these have a different purpose, which we'll get into in the next lesson, but just know these are the high-level choices that you have for regular expressions inside Tableau. Next up is your target field. Your target field is essentially a Tableau field or parameter that is the subject of the regular expression. It's essentially the field that you are going to be searching in to find the text that you need. Next, we have metacharacters. Metacharacters are denoted by a character proceeded by a backslash. These determine the matching logic that you'll be leveraging in your RegEx expression. Some examples are a period which will match any character, \d will match any digit,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=95)** \w will match any [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md), \s will match whitespace, \t will match a tab, and \n will match a new line. What you're essentially doing is laying out all the conditions you want to fulfill to get a match. So each piece that we're going to go through now is a different piece of the text that you want to match. You could want to match everything with a digit and a space or a digit, a space, and a special character. You're really setting up a pattern that's going to search through your data and then return values. In addition to your metacharacters, you have capture groups. Capture groups are denoted by parentheses, and these are used to capture exact word or text matches. This is what you might think of when you're traditionally using a find function. So you might want to find an exact piece of text. If you need to do that, you wrap it in parentheses and it'll find that exact piece. In addition to capture group, we also have character class. Now, character class is denoted by square brackets, and these can be used in place of character literals, which are essentially exact word matches. Some examples might be brackets around a, b, c, which means any of a or b or c. Or another example, a caret in an a, b, c, this would mean not a or b or c. If you've got a range, you could say A to G. This would mean any character between A or G, or you could do the same with numbers.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=190)** In this example, we've got 1 to 3, which would mean any number between 1 and 3. Finally, you have quantifiers. Quantifiers define the acceptable number of previous characters, metacharacters, groups, or sets. In the example shown here, it essentially means that you'll only be accepting values between two and four characters based on the quantifier defined. Now, you're probably looking at this like it's a foreign language at this point, which honestly, it really is. So a quick pro tip when you're learning to handle regular expressions is to get some help. You can get help through tools like RegExr and the ICU Project Userguide. These are both great resources to help you get started with RegEx expressions all the way up to becoming a master. Now, if you're like me, you're probably not going to become a master at regular expressions, and you might just want to look at some examples and get what you need and piece it together for a specific business use case that you're working on. And that's totally fine. That's how most people learn to use tools initially, and then they gradually become better and better at the tool itself. Let's take a quick look at RegExr so that we can give you an idea of what tools can help you along with things like regular expressions and how you can leverage them inside Tableau. All right, so now we're on [regexr.com](https://regexr.com), and this is a free open-source website that basically allows you to test out regular expression code
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=283)** and see what the results are. This was tremendously helpful to me personally when I started learning regular expressions so I could leverage them inside Tableau Desktop. And it's also very helpful that it's free and it has a great community support behind it. Now, one of the really nice things about RegExr is that it can give you some cheat sheet options when you're learning the tool. So if I click on Cheatsheet, it'll give me a bunch of those different references that I just talked about and more to help you get up and running. One of the best pieces of RegExr is the community patterns. So I can click on Community Patterns and I can see what are the most popular patterns that people have submitted publicly, and can I leverage those? So if you're thinking of trying to find a specific piece of text, maybe someone already thought of that and developed a piece of RegEx code that you can leverage, and you can just tweak it a little bit and then use it in Tableau. This is a really great place to do that. You can search through and find what you're looking for. So if I was looking for a password validation code, I could click on Password Validation, I could load up the password validation code, hit OK, and it will give me the code that that user has created, and it'll give me a test piece of text. What I can do here is go in and paste my text from my real data, have this run, and it will show me what the results will be. It gives you a little result window down in the bottom, which explains the different pieces of RegEx code. You can see it highlighting up top as I go through, which is super helpful when you start learning the tool.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=378)** Now, to give you an idea how this might work for you, let me go ahead and just delete everything in the top and the bottom. It's found the email that I was looking for, and it's leveraging my expression up here. I can go down and I can see what pieces of my expression are working. And the nice part is I can use my real data. I can try things out before I bring it into Tableau. Because once it's in Tableau, it's a little bit of a black box as far as if it's working or not, because I won't really get a return if it's just not working. But in RegExr, I can test things out, try them out, try the simplest piece of text first and then add onto it, and I can fine tune it before I bring it into Tableau. #### [Regular expressions functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so now that we've gone through regular expressions at a high level, let's dive into regular expression functions. Regular expressions in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) leverage four predefined functions, which represent the most common RegEx use cases. These include replace, match, extract, and extract_nth. Regular expressions are most useful for business use cases where you need to find, replace, or match specific text. They're also particularly useful if you're doing validation on dimensional values like phone numbers, social security numbers, passwords, and more. If your data is dirty when ingested into Tableau, RegEX can help tease out important pieces of information. This is a great use case if ETL isn't an option for you, if you're leveraging a published source, or generally don't own the data. Let's do a quick overview of each RegEX expression in Tableau, and then we'll jump into Tableau and do some practical application. RegEx replace returns a copy of the given string where the regular expression pattern is replaced by the replacement string. You can think of this as pretty much a find and replace. So you'd set up your calculation RegEx replace. In parentheses, you'd put the text you're looking for. In this example, we've got abc space 123. What we're looking for is a space. So that's why you have that \s right after it. And what you want to replace it with is the dash,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions-functions?u=76281980&t=94)** and that's what you have in the third part of the clause. Your result is abc-123. The match function will return true if a substring of the specified string matches the regular expression pattern. You can think of this pattern as essentially finding your match and then just telling you if it's there or not. It's not doing anything to it. It's just giving you a, boolean, yes, I found it, or, no, I didn't. RegEx extract will return only the portion of the string that matches the regular expression pattern. So in the example we have here, we have abc 123, and then we're defining what we're finding. So we've got our a to z, which covers our abc. We've got \s, which is our space in between. And then after that, we have a capture group. You can see that by the parentheses. And then we've got the \d. The \d will basically tell us to match any digit, and that's why a result is the 123. So the first piece of that clause is just the proceeding values that are going to match, but then the last piece is basically saying capture this. And since this is the extract function, it's pulling that out and presenting it as a value. If we had used this as a match, it would just say true if we ended up getting a match, and if we were using replace, we'd be replacing that field out. Finally, we have RegEx extract_nth. This function will return the portion of the string that matches the regular expression pattern. The substring is matched to the nth capturing group
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regular-expressions-functions?u=76281980&t=190)** where the n is the given index. This function is essentially the same as RegEx extract, but instead of just picking the value after, we're specifically telling it which place we need to pull it from. In this case, it's the second. That's denoted by the two at the end. For all other purposes, the calculation is exactly the same as RegEx extract. #### [Review: Table calculations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-table-calculations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-table-calculations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so before we dive into table calculation use cases, we're going to do a quick review of table calculation functionality. Table calculations are sheet-based computations that only evaluate within a given visualization. That means that table calculations are calculated inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md), not your database, and they're actually calculated at sheet level based on the marks that are visualized in the view. Table calcs affect how values are computed within a visual but don't change that underlying data. They can be created in a few different ways, including quick table Calculations and adding table calculations via menu or creating them via a formula. Considering the order of operations inside Tableau, table calculations pretty much happen last. This makes sense when you consider that table calculations use the values that are rendered to do their calculations off of. You'd want them to happen last because once the values are rendered, the table calculations will take effect. Let's look at the three different options for adding table calculations to your view. First, the quick table calculation. This option is the easiest and most straightforward. You can just click on the pill that you have in your view, go down to Quick Table Calculation, and then choose a common function that you'd like to leverage off of that field. Additionally, you can use the Add Table Calculation function. Now this option will bring up the main dialog box, where you can also choose your calculation types, but you can do more customization
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-table-calculations?u=76281980&t=93)** as far as addressing and partitioning your calculation is concerned. Finally, you can embed table calculations inside calculated fields, and you can define their default properties from a calculation itself. This one is the least transparent and obvious, but helpful if you need to perform more actions than are available with our Quick Table Calculations and the regular Add Table Calculations options. Now that we've talked through table calcs high-level, let's jump into Tableau Desktop and just do a refresher on how to add table calculations using the three different methods that we've shown. All right, so we're back in Tableau Desktop, and we're going to go ahead and make a new sheet. and we'll call this sheet Table Calc Refresher. We're going to go to our main Maven Roasters data source, and we'll look for our Sales measure, which should be in our Sales by Store data source. We're going to select on that, and then we're going to Control + select on Transaction Date. We'll go over to Show Me, and we're going to choose a line chart. So for the first option, we're going to do a quick table calc. So we're just going to right-click on our Sales field, go down to Quick Table Calculations, and add a running total. So now you can see that our values are being added going across using a table calculation. Let's go ahead and drag Sales again to the right of our table calculation Sales field.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-table-calculations?u=76281980&t=186)** We'll right-click on this table calculation, and then we're going to use the Add Table Calculation option. We'll get our dialog box, and we can choose our calculation from here. So we're going to, again, do a running total just to represent this a little easier, but you can see that where you select on cells, Tableau will automatically give you a preview of what's going to happen based on what you're choosing with the table calculation. So this option gives you a little more leeway, a little more options in terms of table calcs, and not much harder than your quick table calc. And we'll take Running Total Dales, drag it out, and drop it into the view. Now these three examples are just a refresher, a baseline for you getting used to table calculations and how they're added. We're not going to use this in our visualization, but this is a good way to get back up to speed with table calculations. Next up, we're going to talk about table calculation, addressing, and partitioning options.

#### [Review: Addressing and partitioning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-addressing-partitioning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-addressing-partitioning?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Next up, let's talk about addressing and partitioning in relation to table calculations. When you leverage table calculations, you must use all dimensions in your level of detail for either partitioning or addressing. Now, what do those topics really mean? Well, if we look at our Table Calculation menu, we can see that we have a field here that is checked. This is one of our addressing fields. Essentially, addressing fields are used to define the direction in which a calculation is evaluated, i.e., down, across then down, across, whereas our partitioning will essentially define the level or group at which the table calculations are evaluated. Is this going to be at a cell level, a table level, or an individual pane level? So that sounds relatively straightforward, but how does that actually work inside the view that you've built? So we can tell in this view that we're using an across then down pattern. That across then down pattern is essentially our addressing field, which, in this case, is our Year of Order Date. You can see the Year of Order Date on the column shelf, and it's going across the top. So that Year of Order Date is defining the direction of our calculation, and we've chosen across then down so you can see that zigzag pattern happening. Now, the box around the outside is our partition, and in this case, we've chosen to partition at the pane level,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-addressing-partitioning?u=76281980&t=92)** which is that individual box where we have the cross section of quarter and year. If we were to do table level, it would expand to the entire table. If we were to do cell level, it would be the individual cell in this spreadsheet. You can think of the partition as a box that our addressing fields will bounce off of. That's why we have this zigzag pattern going across then down. The down is dictated by where we hit the end of the partition and then start over. So a quick pro tip here, you can reorder the addressing fields to change the direction of the calculation. In our example, we only have one addressing field, but in many cases, you have tables that have more than one. Even in this view, we could have had addressing fields for our quarter of our order date and our month of order date, but we haven't chosen to do that. One of the best ways to figure out your addressing and partitioning options is to just play with the data in this menu. The nice part of the Table Calculation menu is that you can select, deselect, and reorder your fields inside that window and get a preview of the data live. So if you really are having a hard time figuring out which options you should choose, just go ahead and choose them and see what comes out. If the values represent what you want them to be, then you know what those settings should be. And the more times you do this, the more you'll get used to setting your addressing fields and your partitioning fields to get the values that you really need.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-addressing-partitioning?u=76281980&t=185)** So now that we've talked about addressing and partitioning high level, let's just jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop quickly. >
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-addressing-partitioning?u=76281980&t=280)** So we have 1, 2, 3, 4, no matter what. So you can see going across, we're doing our ranking across based on table across. We can also do that table down. You can see now we're ranking in this individual column and we'll highlight yellow where that addressing is happening. We could do across then down, right? So it's going across and then down and then across, and then down, that zigzag pattern. Or we could do down and then across. So we're going down and then we're going across, or we could do the individual cell, which will, honestly, result in nonsense, but everything is ranked one when it's compared to itself. Or we could go into our specific dimensions and do our addressing and partitioning fields by hand. So I could click on Quarter of Transaction Date, and that would become our addressing field. Our partitioning field would be Store ID. Now, I could do the reverse and I could drag this Store ID up, click on Store ID, and then Store ID becomes my addressing field and my partitioning field becomes Quarter of Transaction Date. Now, if I go ahead and make both addressing, then that's going to do my rank across all of my individual fields in my table as a whole. So you can see here, you got a lot of different options to play with as far as addressing and partitioning is concerned. You can use the out-of-the-box options or you can customize it with specific dimensions. Next up, we're going to be taking a look at positional table calculation functions.

#### [Position-based table calculations functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/position-based-table-calculations-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/position-based-table-calculations-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at Position-Based Table Calculation Functions. Position-based table calcs are valuable tools for advanced analytics, such as calculating metrics like employee CAGR, also known as Continuous Average Growth Rate. Position-based table calcs allow users to navigate within a table and pull pieces of information for their calculations. This is particularly useful as those values may be difficult to render outside of a [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md)-generated table space. Let's take a look at the CAGR example. Now, here we can see that our CAGR is 19.6%. Below the CAGR, you can see the CAGR calculation, which states that you're going to take the ending value, divide by the beginning value, take that number, and put it to the power of one divided by the number of years between those values. Then, you'll take that, and you'll subtract one. This will give us our Continuous Average Growth Rate over that specified time period. We can leverage table calculations to create a CAGR in the same visualization. The first position-based table calc we'd leverage is called LOOKUP. LOOKUP will return the value of the given expression in a target row. The target row is specified as a relative offset from the current row, and these will be represented in just regular numbers. So you could say minus one for the row prior or positive one for the row in front. The current row is always zero. LOOKUPs become even more powerful when you leverage other position-based table calcs inside it.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/position-based-table-calculations-functions?u=76281980&t=91)** Two calcs are of particular importance. First, we'll return the number of rows from the current row to the first row in the partition. In this visualization, it makes a lot of sense to use FIRST because we're going to want the CAGR to go from our first value to our most current value. So from 2001 all the way up until 2019. So if we leverage LOOKUP in combination with FIRST, we should be able to do that, but we also need the most current value. For that, we can use another position-based table calculation called LAST. LAST will return the number of rows from the current row to the last row in the partition. Note that we'll be leveraging some other table calculation types in the advanced analytics section of this course. Specifically, window standard deviation. Much of what's considered advanced is the application of the calculations rather than the complexity of the calculations themself. Our method of combining the calculations together to get something unique and powerful is really what we consider advanced. Now that we've talked through position-based table calculations high level, let's go ahead and dive into Tableau Desktop and create our own CAGR calculation. #### [Pro tip: Index top filter](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-index-top-filter?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-index-top-filter?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The index function in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) is a versatile table calculation which returns the index for the current row in the current partition. This acts similar to the rank function, which is also a table calculation in Tableau, but it will always rank one, two, three, four. There'll be no replication, or ties, or anything like that. One of the most common ways to leverage index is in place of a TOP function. Now you'll remember from our beginner course that TOP functions essentially allow us to do top tens, top fives, dynamic top selections for dimensions in a view. The problem is often you'll find complications with TOP functions because of our order of operations. So, typically, when you're doing a TOP function, you're doing it at the dimension level. If you look at the order of operations on the left hand side, you can see that's right below context filters and right above measure filters. Now, because some dimensional filters you need to happen before you do your TOP function, traditionally, you're going to have to elevate them to context filters, which can create other complications. But by leveraging index to do your TOP filtering, that will happen at the very end. You can see Table Calc Filters happens last after everything else is calculated. So you won't have to worry about pulling a dimension up to contact to make sure that it's included in the TOP function that you've used because your table calculation filters happen so late in the order of operations. So what this really does is simplify using TOP functions for your view. All you need to do to construct the view is to create an index field, which is simply the index function itself,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-index-top-filter?u=76281980&t=94)** bring it into your rows or columns, make it discreet, and then apply the index using a table calculation. Now, as long as you have this going in the correct direction for your addressing field, it will do your ranking properly. And then once that's completed, you can just add this as a filter and apply the filter to your sheet. Now, if you don't want your index to be overtly displayed in the view, you can just uncheck show header and that will be a hidden pill that you've got in your sheet. The best part is you'll never have to worry about adding anything to context or making sure that something is applying before your index filter, because your index filter happens dead last in the order of operations. So everything else will happen first, including your action filters, your regular dimensional filters, and even your measure filters. This is probably one of the most simple table calculations you can use, but it's often the most powerful, especially for troubleshooting your TOP functions. If your TOP functions aren't working correctly or if you simply want to take a shortcut, using an index is a great way to go. Now, index has other use cases for distributing your marks and other things like that, but in this case we're just going to use it to do our positional ranking and we're going to filter off of that. So let's go ahead and dive in Tableau Desktop and create our own index TOP filter. All right, back in Tableau Desktop. We're going to go to our Maven Roasters dashboard and then we're going to open up our filter using our Alt + Click
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-index-top-filter?u=76281980&t=188)** and then we're going to change our chart that we're showing to our bars and we'll go down and we're going to go to our bars view. So we can just go to the sheet here. And this is where we're going to be adding our index TOP filter. So now we need to make our index calculation. We can just right click create calculated field. We're just going to call this index and we literally just need to add the index function. Everything else will take care of itself once we bring the field into the view, we just need to make sure it's calculating in the correct order. So let's hit OK and then we can take our index and we're going to drag it to rows. Initially this will come out as a continuous field. We can just right click and make this discreet. And once that's discreet, we'll just drag it over to the left hand side because we want this to be the first thing that does the ranking. And right now it looks like it's ranking correctly, it's calculating table down, and it's going one, two, three, four, five, which is what we want. So, theoretically, if we created a top filter off of this, we could just compare against this value and make sure that the value that people are selecting will filter out the data. And to control that top filter, we're going to use a parameter. So let's right click and create a parameter, and we'll call this parameter Top N, N being any value that we want to pick. We'll choose integer. For now, we'll change the default to 10, just in case there's a lot of values when we change multidimensions. And we'll choose OK. Next up, we're going to create our filter calculation. So we're going to right click create calculated field,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/pro-tip-index-top-filter?u=76281980&t=283)** and we're going to call this field TOP N Filter. And this is going to be a simple Boolean field. We're really just taking our index field so we can Control + Click and drag that in. We're taking our index field and we're comparing it against the TOP N parameter. So we're just going to say the index is less than or equal to the TOP N parameter. So we can tell this index is a regular field because it's orange, the parameter is purple, and we can also see that here we could drag it in or just type it in. So now all we need to do is hit OK on this calculation and drag this over to our filter shelf. And we don't have to worry about order of operations or anything like that 'cause that's all taken care of naturally in Tableau. The Table Calc Filter will happen dead last, which is what we want. Let's go ahead and click custom value list and we're going to type in true, we could have selected true on the select from list, but runs the risk of just selecting everything once that false pops in. Let's go to product and see what happens. #### [Review: Level of detail expressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-level-of-detail-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-level-of-detail-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, first up, we're going to do a review of level detail expressions. Then we'll dive into some specific business use case applications of LODs, including customer order frequency, cohort analysis, and new customer acquisition. Now, what exactly are LOD expressions? LOD expressions allow users to control the granularity at which a calculation is computed. Traditionally calculations are computed at the level of detail shown in the view. So depending on the dimensions that I bring it out into the view, those will impact the level of detail of the view and as such, the aggregation that occurs to our measures. LOD expressions are an exception to that rule. So we're able to control at which level our calculations are done, regardless of whatever's included in the view. Now that brings up a good question of order of operations. Our traditional order of operations puts our extract filters, then our data source filters, our context filters, dimension measure, and table calcs in order. So where do LODs fit in this? Our fixed LODs land in between our dimension filters and our context filters. They sit along with our conditional filters, our sets, and our top end calculations. This means that the only thing that can supersede a fixed LOD is a context filter, at least from what we can control within an individual sheet. Now, there's two other types of level detail calculations, which are include and exclude.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-level-of-detail-expressions?u=76281980&t=93)** So our include and exclude LODs will happen right after our dimension filters, at the same level that our data blending occurs. These are important pieces of context to have in your mind as we're going through examples of fixed, include, and exclude, and as we're working through business use case scenarios. You'll always want to have an accurate position of where your calculations are happening in the order of operations as you're working through them. That way you can make sure that your calculations are being done at the exact right level of detail. Now let's take a quick look at the calculation syntax for LODs. So here's a simple fixed LOD expression. We're saying fixed at the state level. I want to take the sum of sales. Now this first part is called the level of detail element. This specifies which LOD you're using. We'll dive into the differences between LOD types in the next lesson, but just know the first piece that you're going to put in is the type of LOD you're going to use. The second piece is called your dimension declaration. This essentially defines the grain at which the data is going to be aggregated. Now, in this calculation, I'm saying that I want this to happen at state level, so I put in my state dimension. The final piece is your aggregate expression. Now, this always has to be an aggregate of some kind, so you need to remember that you're always going to have to aggregate whatever measure you're putting in here or if you're doing account distinct of a dimension, that would also go on the right-hand side of this calculation. Whichever aggregation you put,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/review-level-of-detail-expressions?u=76281980&t=186)** that will happen at the dimension declaration level. So at a high level, LODs are relatively simple in their construction. You have your level of detail element, which LOD you're using, you have the dimensions that you're declaring you want your aggregation to happen at, and then you have your aggregation. Now, that's not to say that LODs aren't complex, but as long as you understand the general syntax and where they're happening in the order of operations, you'll definitely be able to get the hang of leveraging LODs in your everyday analysis life. Now that we're done our high level review of LOD expressions, our next lesson is going to dive into which LOD type you should choose, either include, exclude, or fixed.

#### [Choosing an LOD type](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about choosing a level of detail expression type. Now, LOD expressions come in three flavors. Each has their own way of defining the granularity at which the calculation will be performed. These include Fixed, Exclude, and Include. Now, let's, for a moment, consider the view to the right. Let's imagine we've created a sheet which we'll call our view, and that sheet has a standard level of detail given to it by the dimensions placed inside the view. We're going to look at how each LOD expression manipulates the level of detail within its calculation. First, let's take a look at the Exclude LOD. Exclude LODs are calculated at a coarser or higher grain than the view. These calculations are impacted by filters within the view. The best way to describe Exclude calculations is simply that it's going to remove a dimension from the view, but only for the calculation itself, not the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the view. So, if your view had state and city and you chose to exclude the city in your calculation, the calculation would only calculate at the state level, essentially following this arrow going up and creating a coarser grain or more aggregated state for the calculation. That value would then be repeated on all the records shown in the view at that higher-level grain and that higher-level aggregation,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=92)** essentially ignoring the dimension that you've chosen to exclude. Include LODs are calculated at a finer or lower grain than the view. Just like Exclude LODs, these calculations are also impacted by filters within the view. If you imagine a view that has state shown but not city and you were to create a calculation using the Include function that includes city, that would create a finer grain, a less aggregated state for your calculation. So your calculation would calculate at city level and then it would aggregate up to the level of detail of the view. Note the two differences here between Includes and Excludes and how they represent in the view. Excludes will repeat their values, but Includes will aggregate up to the level of the view after their calculation is completed. The third and final option is a Fixed LOD. Now a Fixed LOD is a bit different. It's calculated at either a finer or coarser grain than the view. While the Include and Exclude calculations will take into account the other dimensions in the view that they aren't specifically excluding or including, Fixed LODs will not. Fixed LODs will only consider the dimensions called out in the calculation. They'll ignore all other dimensions in the view, with the exception being if you have something on your filter shelf that is placed in context, that will be the equivalent of calling out that dimension in your Fixed LOD.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=186)** Other than that, the Fixed LOD concentrates on what you defined in the calculation only. As such, these calculations aren't impacted by filters within the dashboard, unless they're placed into context. What this means is that you can make a Fixed LOD perform like an Exclude function or like an Include function, but it will differ in that the other dimensions not mentioned in the calculation will be ignored, whereas those dimensions do impact the Include and Exclude calculations, as long as it's not specifically excluded. Another note is that your Include and Exclude calculations are always measures. Your Fixed calculation can either be a dimension or a measure, but it will inherit the aggregates properties that you've selected in the aggregation piece of your LOD. A quick pro tip is that there is a fourth option, but it's essentially a Fixed LOD that has no dimensions called out. This is called a table-scoped level of detail calculation. Now, for this type of calculation, all you need to do is add curly brackets around an aggregation, and [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) will translate that to a fixed calculation with no dimensions called out. Table-scoped calcs are a nice way to do a shorthand fixed calculation that's not calling out any dimensions. These can be particularly useful when you just want to find an overall max or min or some sort of simple calculation that you want it to ignore everything else
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=279)** and just find the max or min in the dataset. You can use table-scoped to perform other actions in addition to these. Now that we've talked through the different level of detail expression types, let's jump into Tableau Desktop and go through an example of each. Let's go ahead and create our base sheet, where we'll be able to test our LOD calculations out. We're going to click on New Sheet and we'll just call this LOD Expressions. Then, we'll select some of our fields. Let's go to Sales by Store, we'll select Store Id, and then we need to choose Transaction Date and Sales. So let's Control + Click on Transaction Date, Control + Click on Sales. We'll go to Show Me, and we want this to be a text table initially. Next, we're going to take our Year of Transaction Date, drag it to the right of Store Id, and expand it to be at quarter level. We're going to go ahead and change our sum of sales to be average sales. This is the average sale at the order line level. All right, so first up, we're going to create our Include LOD expression. We'll right-click and choose Create Calculated Field. We'll call this LOD - Include. And let's start typing our calculation. So we're going to say INCLUDE, and then we're going to do the month of the transaction date. So this'll be the dimension that we're focusing on. We're actually bringing month level into the view, where we normally just have store ID, the year, and the quarter.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=373)** Then we'll do our aggregation, which is going to be the average sales, and we'll end off with a curly bracket. Now, let's see what this does when we bring it into the view. We'll go ahead and we'll double-click on the LOD - Include. And you can see our aggregation is a bit different. You can see that initially, it's doing a sum of our LOD. Now, this is allowing us to do an aggregation on top of an aggregation, which normally isn't possible inside Tableau. You can see we're doing the average inside and we're doing it at the month level. And then we're also able to do another aggregation on top of that average. Now, if I end up getting my average at the month level and adding those three months that make up this quarter, I'm going to get this 13.765. But if I take the average across those months, that will give me a different value than what's displayed in the normal average sales. This value of the Average LOD Include is actually giving me the average of the average of those three months within the quarter, instead of just the overall total average order line level sales dollars. Now, let's go ahead and see what an Exclude can do for us with this view. First off, let's find our regular Sales field, and we'll just drag that in right next to the Include 'cause we're now going to be looking at whole dollars. So just a regular sum. Let's right-click and create another calculated field

> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/choosing-an-lod-type?u=76281980&t=467)** that we're going to call LOD - Exclude. Our Exclude calculation will start with EXCLUDE, and then we need to call out a dimension. In this case, we're going to remove quarter from this calculation. So we're going to EXCLUDE DATEPART ('quarter',[TransactionDate]), and then we'll call out our aggregation, which will, again, be SUM([Sales]). Let's go ahead and close the bracket there. And now, we have our complete Exclude LOD. So this, again, is saying, "We want to do the sum of sales, but we don't want to do it at the quarter level. We want to pretend that quarter doesn't exist in this view at all, and we're going to aggregate up to the year." So, let's go ahead and click OK. And then we're just going to bring our next LOD right next to this new Sales field. So if you bring this out, you can clearly see that we have 558,849, which is the summation of each one of these quarters together. Now, with Exclude LODs, like we talked about, it's going to repeat the value at our different records shown in the view. So you can see that 558 repeated on each line, and that number correlates to all of 2017. Now, let's try out a Fixed calculation. Let's right-click Create Calculated Field. We're going to call this LOD - Fixed and then we're going to create our fixed calculation. We can just start by typing FIXED and hitting Enter. We want to fix on store ID

So now, our 1.4 million is actually the total sales for the entire store. So not only are we ignoring these two dimensions, but we're specifically calling out store ID because that's where we want our level to aggregate to. Now, one final example, let's right-click Create Calculated Field and call this LOD - Table Scoped. #### [LOD use case: Benchmarking and time flags](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Benchmarking using LOD expressions allows users to isolate certain dimensions or measures for comparative analysis. Along with benchmarking, time flags can also allow for dynamic benchmarking dates to be applied to measures. Benchmarking uses a combination of LODs and nested IF or CASE statements to isolate dimensions and measures for analysis in layered views. Here we can see a prior year benchmark displayed as a dotted line across our main trend line. This can be really useful to have our prior year benchmark while we're comparing our regular year. We can even do calculations versus our prior year benchmark to see how we're comparing on a regular basis. Our benchmark calculation displayed here will isolate the year 2018 sales by nesting an IF statement inside the aggregation portion of a fixed LOD. Once that's aggregated up, we divide by 12 to get the monthly benchmark for last year, and that can be applied as the reference line shown in the above visualization. Now you'll notice that that 2018 is hardcoded and that's not going to work on an ongoing basis, when we roll into next year, next month, or next week. To handle those types of scenarios, we'll leverage time flags. We can create time flags using IF expressions in combination with LOD calculations to allow them to be dynamically updated as data evolves.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=97)** Time flags will need to be dynamic, especially if your data isn't updated daily. There are ways to filter down your views with our relative time filters that [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) provides, but if your data is even a little bit old, those aren't going to work. Today isn't necessarily today in your data. You may only have data updated through yesterday or last week or last month. To handle these types of scenarios, we'll develop time flags using LODs. You can see in our dynamic flag calculation, we've got a fixed LOD, which is just taking the straight max of the year and it's embedding it in our IF statement. So essentially, if our year of our date in our dataset equals the year of the max year, then 1 else 0. We can take this flag and multiply it against any measure in the dataset. Now if the flag condition isn't met and the value is zero, then our sales are effectively zeroed out. But if it is met, we get a one, then we're just multiplying a one against our metric, leaving the this year year-to-date metric available for aggregation. Now we had this flag before, but we had hardcoded that 2019 value. Now that we've added that FIXED MAX of Year ID, that's dynamic. If our data goes over to next year, then the new value would be 2020 and 2021 and so on. So enabling that time flag to be dynamic really frees us up to make this a dashboard that will live on into the future
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=193)** with much less maintenance on our part. Now that we've talked high level through benchmarking and time flags, let's go into our dashboard and apply these in a practical way. All right, so we're back in Tableau desktop. Let's go to our Maven Roasters dashboard. We'll go down and we're going to be looking at our trend view. Let's go ahead and click our year-over-year parameter action here, and then we'll go into our line chart sheet. Next, we'll go ahead and create our calculated field just by right clicking Create Calculated Field, and we'll name this Sales Last Year Benchmark. All right, so let's go ahead and create our sales last year benchmark. Now I'm just going to paste this in so we can speed this up a little bit, and I'll explain what we've done. So in the first piece of this IF statement we're saying, if the selected measure is 1, which is the code for sales, then we are going to do the sum of the fixed calculation of the sum of last year year-to-date divided by 12. Now, what is this second piece doing? Well, if we look inside this calculation, we have the sales last year year-to-date. Now our view is displaying by month. So normally, if we brought out our sales last year year-to-date, it would only show up for last year and then the continuous line would be broken. What we want to do is get the average per month and just have that as a straight line going across this dataset. So since we have our sales last year year-to-date
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=288)** inside our fixed LOD, that's essentially ignoring all of these other measures and dimensions in the View and just getting the last year's total sales, then I'm dividing by 12, and we're presenting the value. Since our fixed isn't calling anything out, again, it's just going to ignore everything else and we'll be all set with our benchmark. Of course, if these conditions aren't met, we're not going to show any values and that's all we need to do. Let's hit Okay. Let's go ahead and search for benchmark. We'll drag that into the right hand side of this View, drop it in, and then we'll do a couple changes. First off, we're going to right click on our axis and choose Synchronize Axis. So that'll be going across, that's looking right. Next, we're going to go to all for our marks, and we're going to pull off measure names because we really don't want that to be those two colors. And we have the value now, which is good.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=381)** Looks like we need to do a little cleanup on the format. Our Sales Last Year Benchmark is now in dollars, thousands. If we hover over, forgot the K there, thousands. And that's looking good. So now we have our benchmark, we have our actual. So now that we have our dollars and thousands, that's looking good. So we have our benchmark value, we have our actual value. What we might want to do is just change our tooltip a slight amount, and instead of the month, let's just hardcode this to 2018 Benchmark for now. And that will be a reminder to the end user that this is not a different sales value that they should be tracking on an ongoing basis. It's just a benchmark line that they need to adhere to when they're doing their analysis. All right, so now that our benchmark is in place, we need to go ahead and modify our time flags. So let's go ahead and look for our time flags. I'm just going to search flag, there we go. We've got this year and last year. So let's go into this year. And we need to replace this 2019 to be dynamic. So we're going to go ahead and start typing FIXED. We're not going to call anything out, we just want the FIXED :MAX of the entire dataset. And we're going to do Year ID here. So we can just close our bracket,
>
> **[7:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3951024?u=76281980&t=476)** do a curly bracket at the end, and now this will always pick the fixed max year in the entire dataset to compare against instead of just 2019, which is what this dataset goes through right now. If this data were to get updated for 2020, 2021, then it would pick up the new max year based on this dynamic time flag that we've created. So let's just go ahead, I'm just going to copy this 'cause we're going to need it for the other fields as well. So we'll hit Okay here, and then we'll right click on Last Year Year-To-Date, choose Edit. And instead of 2019, we're going to want the same calculation, which is the FIXED :MAX of Year ID, but instead, we're going to do minus 1, and that will be last year, or the year before the current max. Let's choose Okay on that. And now, both of our year-to-date this year flags are dynamic and we'll go on even when we roll into next year. #### [LOD use case: Customer order frequency](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950022?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950022?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Our next LOD use case centers around customer order frequency. Now, customer order frequency leverages LODs to segment customers into buckets for further analysis or targeting, ie, marketing, site optimization, promotions and more. This analysis is particularly useful for identifying your best and most loyal customers as well as those who may need more attention to boost their stickiness. Let's take a look at our example view. If we look at this view, we've got our regular count of customer ID, which is giving us our total count of customers, and then we have a Pareto, which is showing our percent of total customers in running fashion. Now, the field that we're concentrating on here, that's an LOD, is our orders per customer. Orders per customer will fix on customer ID. And this will allow us to segment our order frequency into buckets that can be analyzed as bars. So once our LOD is complete, we can create those bins based on how many times customers have ordered and then we can group them together for further analysis. Our second dual axis piece of this visualization is our Pareto style nested table calculation, which isn't related to LODs but is an added benefit from the last section that we talked about related to table calculations. It's essentially a running total with a percent of totals stacked on top of it. This helps add depth to the magnitude
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/3950022?u=76281980&t=92)** of orders per customer metric because we can tell not only how many customers fall into that one order bucket, but what percent of total that represents inside the entire dataset. Let's go ahead and jump into [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) desktop and create our customer order frequency analysis, leveraging LODs. Alright, so we're back in Tableau desktop and we're going to create a new sheet. This sheet is going to be called LOD-Customer Order Frequency. We're going to go ahead and create a calculated field and we'll call that orders per customer. The calculation will be a simple fixed calculation, so we'll say fixed on the customer ID. So now we're going to do count D of our order field and we'll close that with a regular parenthesis and a curly bracket. We'll click okay and we'll have our orders per customer. We'll go ahead and double click our orders per customer and we can see that has one through eight, that's right, different buckets of multiple orders per customer. #### [Predictive analytics](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/predictive-analytics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/predictive-analytics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md), in its simplest terms, helps us determine the likelihood of future outcomes by applying advanced analytic techniques to historical data. Predictive analytics is a hugely expansive topic of which we can only cover a fraction of in this course. As a result, we'll be focusing on covering tools internal to [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) for the most part, as well as introducing some external and third party tools like R, [Python](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Python%20(Programming%20Language).md) and MATLAB. These tools should be a good starting point for those individuals looking to dip their toe into the lake that is predictive analytics and [Data Science](../../Topics/Data%20Science.md) in Tableau. Note that we understand there is much deeper integration and use cases to be explored in concert with Tableau and invite developers to explore the different techniques and options after this course. As mentioned, we'll be going through both internal and external tools. Tableau provides several out of the box predictive analytic tools, including regression, shown as trendlines, [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md), control charts, and cluster analysis. These will be our focus for the internal tools lessons. Tableau also integrates with third party programming languages and tools, including Python, R, MATLAB, and more. Our hope is that exposing you to both the internal and external tools will kickstart your journey with predictive analytics inside Tableau. We'll kick off internal tools in the next lesson by looking at regression models inside Tableau.

#### [Regression models](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regression-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regression-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So before we dive into practical application of [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) Desktop, we're going to talk high level about regression models, their purpose, and their application inside Tableau. Regression models are one of the most fundamental predictive analytics tools used wherever [Data Science](../../Topics/Data%20Science.md) is found. Regression models in Tableau are represented in the form of trend lines. Regression models are statistical models used to estimate the trend or relationship between variables. They involve a dependent variable and one or more independent or explanatory variables, whose impact on the dependent variable we are trying to uncover. There are five different types of trend lines or regression models that can be used in Tableau, and each has a different purpose. A [Linear Regression](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Linear%20Regression.md) model is the simplest model that estimates the relationship between two variables, either increasing or decreasing. Linear regressions are best used when the data resembles a linear pattern. For example, if one loaf of bread costs $2, then four loafs should cost $8. If you are at all in doubt, which regression model or trend line you should use in Tableau, start with a linear regression, review the results and see if it makes sense. More often than not, linear regression is the right way to go initially. If you've got a clear differentiating shape to your data, you may want to leverage one of the other four regression models. Logarithmic regression model features curved lines
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regression-models?u=76281980&t=95)** of best fit that increase quickly and then level off gradually. You should avoid logarithmic trends when values are negative. An example of a good fit for a regression model would be the efficiency of warehouse workers over time. In this example, the efficiency would go up very quickly initially, and then it would level off over time as workers become maximum efficient at their tasks. Exponential regression models feature curve lines of best fit, most suitable when rates of change between variables rise at increasingly higher rates. You should avoid exponential trends for negative values. These, like the logarithmic regression models, will be filtered out. One of the most prominent examples of exponential regression models is computing power of microchips. Defined by Moore's law, it's relatively common knowledge that the computing power of microchips doubles approximately every two years. This creates an exponential trend in the data. Polynomial regressions feature curved lines of best fit, most suitable when variables have fluctuating relationships. For this model, your data may appear to have a bounce in it or a curved line that goes up, and then down, and then up again. An example of this model could be revenue over time for a new product launch, sales would have an initial bump, a dip, and then another curve going up as the product stabilizes and becomes more popular. A power regression model features curve lines of best fit, most suitable when dependent variables increase
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/regression-models?u=76281980&t=189)** at a predetermined rate. You should avoid power trends for negative values, as they will also be filtered out, much like some of the other regression models we've talked about. An example that most people are probably familiar with from their youth is the trajectory of skipping a stone on water over time. Next up, we'll jump into the practical application of these regression models using trend lines in Tableau.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) (6), [Linear Regression](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Linear%20Regression.md) (3), [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) (2), [Data Science](../../Topics/Data%20Science.md) (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Trend lines](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/trend-lines?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/trend-lines?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this lesson, we're going to be going through trend lines, which are the practical application of regression models inside [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Trend lines reveal the prevailing direction or line of best fit for a series of data points. Trend lines can be especially useful when your data is noisy or when the trend isn't immediately clear. We should note that trend lines require two continuous variables. If you're using time on your x-axis, then it'll need to be a continuous pill. Without two continuous variables, you'll notice that your trend line option won't be available for your review. We can see a trend line in the example below, and adding trend lines is relatively straightforward. All you'll need to do is go to your Analytics tab, drag Trend Line, and drop it in. When you drop it in, you're going to be selecting which regression model you're going to use, and the trend line will automatically be applied. The tricky piece of trend lines and regression models is that they're easy to apply in Tableau, but it can be difficult to tell if they're accurate. To help with this, we can look at two key values given to us when we put a trend line into the view. These include R-Squared and P-Value. They can be found inside your trend line tooltip, or in your Explain Model option, which will showcase your model coefficients and more. The R-squared value explains how the variance of one variable explains the variance of another, ranging from zero to one. The closer you get to one, the more accurate that prediction is.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/trend-lines?u=76281980&t=93)** For example, if your R-Squared is equal to 0.5, then only half of the observed variation can be explained by the model's inputs. The other key value that we should be concentrating on is called P-Value. P-Value is the [Probability](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Probability.md) of obtaining results that are as extreme as the observed results. P-Value can range from zero to one as well. However, the closer you are to zero with P-Value, the more accurate your model is. A P-Value of less than 0.05 is a common standard for model accuracy and means that your trend line is a strong predictor for future data points. Now that we've talked through applying trend lines and how to tell if they're accurate, let's jump into Tableau Desktop and apply some of our own. All right, so we're back in Tableau Desktop in our main workbook, and we're on our Maven Roasters Detail tab. Now, the first thing we're going to need to do is bump up the size of this dashboard because our [Predictive Analytics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Predictive%20Analytics.md) visualizations are going to take up quite a bit more space than we already have in our 1,400 by 1,400 pixel dashboard. Let's go over to Custom size and choose a height of 2,500. Now, before you do this, you're going to notice that your visualizations that we've already put inside the dashboard are going to stretch out. #### [Forecasting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/forecasting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/forecasting?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Now let's take a look at [Forecasting](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Forecasting.md) in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md). Forecast [Algorithms](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Algorithms.md) evaluate patterns in historical data to project potential future data points. Tableau provides several drag and drop forecasting tools inside the analytics pane. It should be noted that Tableau uses something called exponential smoothing in their forecasting. Exponential smoothing, iteratively forecasts future values of a time series from weighted average values from the past. Tableau will automatically select the best eight models and will detect seasonality at the grain presented inside the view. Now, adding a forecast to a view is relatively simple in Tableau. All you need to do is go to your analytics pane and drag out your forecast. There's only one option to choose here, and that's just to add your forecast to your view. Now that being said, the forecasting option will be grayed out if the conditions aren't met for forecasting inside Tableau. This includes your data grain, your type, and how much historical data you have. Once your forecast is added in, you can customize it as needed. You'll be able to choose how long forecast will extend. Your automatic option will choose a best fit based on Tableau's algorithms, but you also have the options to choose exactly how much of a forecast length you need or until a certain point. You also have several options related to your source data. Aggregate buy will give you date granularity options down to the second.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/forecasting?u=76281980&t=94)** You can also ignore a defined number of data points and fill missing values with zero. Your forecasting model itself can also be customized inside your forecasting options menu. While automatic is the default, you've also got a few other options. Automatic without seasonality. We'll still choose the automatic model, but it'll remove the seasonality portion. If you select custom, you'll be able to adjust your trend and seasonality options. For both you can either choose additive or multiplicative. Additive will result of contributions of the model being summed while multiplicative will result with contributions being multiplied. You'll also be given the option to show or hide your prediction intervals. Some other key conditions when developing forecasts in Tableau is that there are some restrictions and things to know to be able to generate a proper forecast. Exact dates are not supported in Tableau for forecasting. You should use truncated dates like Feb 2017 or other continuous date parts, like year, year plus quarter, and more. At least five data points are required for a forecast and at least two seasons to leverage seasonality. In addition, you'll need at least 24 months worth of data for a 12 month seasonal cycle. Now that we've talked through adding and customizing forecast, let's jump into Tableau desktop and apply a forecast of our own. Alright, so we're back in Tableau desktop
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/forecasting?u=76281980&t=188)** and we're on our picking duration trend and forecast tab. We're going to go ahead and go to the analytics tab. We'll see forecast, drag it out and drop it on forecast. Now we've got a forecast for both our actual and our estimate, and we want to remove that from our view. So all we need to do is drag this forecast indicator off of our marks card, and we'll have just that one forecast that we're looking for identified. Next, we're going to right click on our forecast and we're going to edit it. Let's go to forecast options and see what we want to change. We're going to go down and change our forecast model to be custom, and we're going to go down and change our trend and our seasonal options to additive. Alright, that's looking much better. We're going to click okay, and now we have our forecast integrated into our view, along with our trend line. To see more detail on our forecast, we can always right click on our forecast and say describe forecast, and we can get some high level details and metrics related to our forecast summary. We can see that we're ignoring the last month, that we're doing a 12 month seasonal pattern cycle, and that we're forecasting forward 15 months into the future. Next up, we're going to be diving into control charts in Tableau.

#### [Control charts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/control-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/control-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Control charts are a statistical tool designed to monitor manufacturing or business processes to ensure they're in a state of control, essentially within the limits that the business sets. Control charts can easily be generated in [Tableau](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Tableau.md) using reference bands. Now typically, the limits for control charts are placed at three standard deviations from the mean. This is often called the normal distribution and covers 99.73% of all data points. Anything outside the band is called special cause variation and should be investigated. Control charts have many vital business use cases for production, manufacturing, and other repetitive tasks. The faster and more efficient the process, the lower overall cost for the business. Adding reference bands in Tableau is relatively straightforward. All you need to do is go to the analytics pane, choose reference band and drag it into the view. Now, you'll be able to choose if you want that applied at a table, pane, or cell level, and you'll also be able to choose which measures you're going to be applying the bands against. Now when doing a control chart, you typically have a horizontal running time-based chart, and you're going to be applying your control bands off of the Y-axis measure. You'll be able to customize your reference bands in the edit reference line band or box menu. To apply a control chart style view, you'll just need to change your values
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-tableau-desktop/control-charts?u=76281980&t=94)** to minus and plus three standard deviations. You'll be able to customize many different options inside your edit reference line band or box menu. This includes the scope, either entire table pane or cell, the value, percentage, percentile, quantile or standard deviation. You can control your labels, either no labels, the computation you're choosing, the value, or a custom combination. You can modify the tool tip to be automatic, custom, or none at all. You'll be able to modify the formatting of the lines and the fill, and you'll be able to choose if you want your bands to be recalculated based on the user's selection.
